B004J4WNL2|4.0|I'm not sure how an extrovert would feel about this book but as a dyed-in-the-wool introvert I really appreciate its existence.  Susan Cain mostly follows the Malcom Gladwell template by illustrating points she is making through interviews and anecdotes.  Many authors use this style, some better than others, and Cain falls on the better side of the spectrum.<br /><br /> I am in a leadership role in my job and every time I take one of those personality tests that are so popular in leadership trainings I feel as if I need to apologize, as if I can't be a strong leader and introvert at the same time.  \\"Quiet\\" vindicates introverts everywhere. I appreciated Cain's writing style, her use of research, and the message she was communicating.<br /><br />The early chapters were my favorite.  I enjoyed how Cain described the transition in the United States from a culture that venerated character to one that lionizes personality over everything else.  She uses the rise of Dale Carnegie to illustrate her point.<br /><br />Chapter two discusses that cult of personality today, from Tony Robbins' seminars to Harvard business school.  Cain uses these examples to illustrate how the extroverted personality has been exalted while introverts are disdained or ignored.  Of course this is just the set-up, subsequent chapters discuss such topics as: achievements which are better accomplished by working alone and how sometimes it is necessary to fake extroversion in order to be successful.  Each chapter highlights research on introversion and similar personality traits and then gives examples of people who embody the theme of the chapter.  Some of these featured personalities include Steve Wozniak of Apple Computer fame, Eleanor Roosevelt and Warren Buffet.  The book eventually runs out of steam and it feels as if Cain is stretching to find chapter topics by the end but overall \\"Quiet\\" is a great read!

0805095535|5.0|not since the works of thomas wolfe, the north carolina writer, have lists of this magnitude been accumulated by a novelist.  like wolfe, paul auster is obsessed with the individual being situated in time passing within the singular body.  auster tallies his pains experienced through out the sixty-four years of his existence, sexual trysts with prostitutes, one who recites baudelaire, entries of the twenty-one addresses where  he's resided since birth, a repertoire of acts that can be done by the human hand, and everything else that he can recall. in an entry about a locale in france, of which he wrote a book, he informs us, much of what he experienced was omitted, so he rhapsodizes in a three page long sentence, all that he can recall from that time.  if you're thinking of marcel proust, then you're in the right territory, auster is a francophile, having lived several years in france, written a biography on the irishman who wrote in french, samuel beckett,  edited an anthology of french poetry, translated joubert and mallarm√© and writes in a style which reminds me of english translations of several of sartre's works, including the philosopher's memoir Words.<br /><br />a journal, written on the east coast, in the borough of brooklyn, ny, in the winter of 2010-2011, the season those of us living on the east coast then, wondered if the snow would ever end, as we woke, expecting almost daily, a new snowfall--as good as any time to write a journal, snowbound, urban cabin fever, approaching sixty-four.<br /><br />writers tend to be squirrelly with their journals, preferring to let the novels, the essays, the memoirs, speak for them, unless journal writing is the preferred literary form.  even then it's a risky undertaking; among the published, few journals attract interest--Speak Memory by nabokov of course, though too stylized to be called a journal, the journals of andre gide are more respected than read, and i know of no one, other than renata adler, who has read any of  samuel pepys' diary.<br /><br />paul auster is a living author with a collection of likable books in most of the usual forms, the novel, the essay, the poem, and the biography. but i don't believe the scholars are yet after his marginalia and ephemera, as with hemingway or henry james.  maybe his fans have been waiting to read what he's been jotting down about his accidents and incidentals in raw form.<br /><br />well here it is.  at some point this book was intended and submitted for publication, subjected to the care of writer and editorial staffs, so it's not too raw.  actually, it's very finished.  his voice is second person, he addresses himself, instead of as the grammatically expected `I', as `You' a pronoun that does not manage to avoid his post-modern musing--satisfying our inner teacher plea for credibility, he shares a fragment of poetry by keats in which the poet refers to himself in the second person, and he recalls those narrators of tough guy hardboiled novels who followed leads and solved cases in the second person.<br /><br />half way through his journal he writes of the death of his mother and reconstructs what he can of her life, including his panic attacks for which he finds an analogy in a ten page synopsis of the 1950 film, D.O.A. (a remake of the film starring dennis quaid and meg ryan appeared in 1988).<br /><br />at sixty-three and sixty-four, mortality is much on his mind, the kinds of death, death by lightning, death by murder, death by accidents in the home, death by war, `absurd and nonsensical' deaths--the french writer, camus, another journal writer, wrote of absurdity and death.<br /><br />at sixty-three and sixty-four, the question is not what can one hold on to faced with the knowledge of the coming of death, but what has one held on to, and on goes auster's `catalogue of sensory data', of his impressions and the connections to events, activities, objects, and persons, a coda of anecdotes, each one inspired by the previous piece, a veritable scheme of variations on a theme: memory.<br /><br />how he met his wife, siri hustvedt, intellectual and author, her books are more at home among the academies of france and germany, than here in the usa, his remarks about her read like information for a curriculum vitae.  writing of her family and their roots and life style and their traditional christmas dinner reminds him of the food he's consumed during a lifetime, which he approaches with the exuberance of the hungry henry miller in paris.<br /><br />journals are a personal undertaking.  paul auster has allowed us to read his.  you might say, he insists.  what more is expected of us, what more can we do, but to  read it and thank him.<br /><br />a rating between 4 and 5 stars.

B0009AM5I6|5.0|This is the recording that turned Angela Gheorghiu into a star virtually overnight and successfully launched her career. It captured the debut performance of La Traviata, not only of the young singer, but also that of the veteran conductor Georg Solti.<br /><br />Combined with a totally traditional production of the opera by Richard Eyre, sumptuous costumes and sets and a fine recording by the experienced Decca team, this 1994 presentation has long been a benchmark recording and one that is still hard to match, let alone beat.<br /><br />One could enlarge upon additional features that add to the merits of this recording. Certainly one should mention the quality of the supporting lead roles. Those of Lopardo as Alfredo and Nucci as his father are outstanding with strong characterisation, sure technique and firm voices. The remaining supporting roles are all done very well indeed and the whole opera is conveyed with tremendous dramatic sweep and certainty progressing remorselessly from light to dark.<br /><br />Not least of all, the production is believable because everyone is of the right age for their parts. This is by no means always true of operas in general, a feature which can crucially undermine the vital `suspension of disbelief' so necessary in all drama.<br /><br />The recording still remains acceptable within the context of its date. The DTS is good re-processed stereo. The image is 4:3 rather than widescreen. The use of spotlighting in the production, especially in the increasingly dark settings in the second half of the opera, can create harsh lighting and burn-out on light details such as light coloured clothing. This is most apparent on long shots which also lack the sharp definition of today's HD cameras. It is best, therefore, to consider this nowadays as an historic recording in terms of technology. This allows for a great deal more latitude than that which would apply to a new recording.<br /><br />So as an historic issue this remains justifiably famous because of all its unique and special qualities which conspire to make this a truly memorable occasion. The recording is fully up to the task on those terms. So on those terms again, I am sure that most people would be happy with a 5 star rating for this very special event.<br /><br />........................................<br /><br />Some dialogue from the comments section that may offer further help:<br /><br />Ian, Ian:<br />Your Reviews are becoming incrementally Better and Better, and this could well be your best ever!<br />Frankly, I find it an Intellectual and Musical delight to read them, even if I have no intention of ever buying another DVD, or 4:3 Aspect Ratio Production.<br />Thank you. The following description therefore is simply intended to be more of a guide to aid personal choice rather than a review leading to a firm conclusion.<br /><br />In this performance, there is a particularly fine balance maintained between the two contrasting characters of Pinkerton and Butterfly which enables them to establish a believable relationship in Act 1. Later in the opera, in Act 3, the impact of Pinkerton's actions seem to finally strike home which enhances the dramatic impact of the opera's conclusion.<br /><br />In this performance Butterfly (Elmira Vida) portrays her unswerving devotion to both Pinkerton (Carlo Barricelli) and her child totally convincingly. The remaining solo parts and chorus are all completely satisfactory.<br /><br />The outdoor set is simple but totally effective. This is a revolving stage showing interior and exterior sides of a Japanese style house with use made of the sliding and translucent panels. These panels enable telling use to be made of shadowy figures such as the arriving and fleeing relatives. Most tellingly it enables an unbending and uncommunicative Kate (Sally Wilson) to be shown in silhouette in contrast to the compliant Suzuki. This totally unyielding posture is maintained to the very end of her part and it makes one fear for the future life of the child in her care.<br /><br />All the parts are well sung and acted with sustained communication with the intended exception of Kate as above. The outdoor nature of the event is overcome by the use of radio mikes which enable every word to be heard clearly. The sound is presented in DTS-HD, Dolby Digital and stereo and is of good quality.<br /><br />In summary therefore, the elements of communication and allied acting in this performance and production combine to achieve a dramatically strong and believable presentation. It is unlikely that it will be considered definitive by many but nevertheless, in my opinion, it would be unreasonable to rate it as worth less than 4 stars. B006CAXPSQ|5.0|Prequel: Amazon has correctly listed this review of Gades Carmen (flamenco dance drama) under Gades but also wrongly under Bizet's Carmen with Alagna.<br />Unfortunately this sort of double listing is quite common as Amazon's listing software seems to get confused by similar titles.<br />Reviewers are unable to do anything about this so please be patient and scroll to the performance that you are interested in.<br />Thanks, Ian Giles.<br /><br />.................................................<br />Carmen flamenco styled dance drama by Gades review<br /><br />This is probably a good place to start investigating the work of Gades as it is such a well-known plot and well told by Bizet in his opera. However this example of the flamenco art combined with more traditional ballet skills as envisioned by Gades is able to extend the rawness of the emotional impact to an almost frightening degree.<br /><br />Some background explaining how this fusion of ballet and flamenco came about may be of help at this stage: Gades, as a young man, studied classical dance as well as all the Spanish popular dance idioms. This dual interest and skill enabled him, uniquely, to fuse together the two art forms of ballet and flamenco. He believed that flamenco must also be shorn of all extraneous distractions in order to bring the essence of the dance to light. At this early stage he was also providing the choreography for classical ballet such as Bolero, Carmen and El Amor Brujo for companies such as La Scala. From 1981 he formed the collaboration with Carlos Saura, the film director, which resulted in some of his flamenco-based creations being filmed. These films had an enormous effect on ballet companies around the world and Gades became very influential in this way while continuing as a ballet dancer in Giselle for example.<br /><br />This production brings the 1983 Carmen to our screens in an amazingly effective performance. The music includes specially pre-recorded Bizet extracts played by the Suisse Romande orchestra with considerable brio. The majority of the music however is pure flamenco - two guitars, solo singers and clapping. Precision ensemble dance to massed multi-rhythmic clapping has to be seen to be believed! The scenarios presented crackle with sexual tensions and competitiveness and an unmistakable underlying and ever-present threat of violence. This is not just between men but also between women and between the sexes. This is an artistic statement beyond any of my previous experiences and I found it gripping. This is not the same as comforting. There is not a moment of tenderness to be seen - not even in the highly disciplined acknowledgement of the final applause.<br /><br />It is likely that non-specialists of this art form will fail to pick up or begin to understand many of the underlying meanings in all of this - subtitles would have been invaluable in the frequent sung parts, but this is not a good reason to deny this full acknowledgement. The recording is all that could be wished for and there is a bonus in the form of interviews with members of the team. The sleeve notes are particularly informative. This issue delivers an unforgettable experience and essential guide to a dramatic art form.le brio. The majority of the music however is pure flamenco - two guitars, solo singers and clapping. B001OBT3FC|5.0|This memorial concert has such close links with Karajan that it is worthwhile explaining why it meant so much to both Ozawa and Mutter. The connection between both of them and between themselves and Karajan is that they were both Karajan's prot&eacute;g&eacute;es and had been considerably supported by Karajan in establishing their respective careers. They therefore make an especially appropriate pair of artistes to fulfil their roles in this concert.<br /><br />The bonus feature is in the form of a post-concert interview with both Mutter and Ozawa separately in which they are asked what effect Karajan had on their lives and also why they had chosen their particular pieces to play/conduct.<br /><br />In the case of Anne-Sophie Mutter she made very clear her admiration for Karajan outlining his many special contributions to music and to music making. In particular she emphasised his abilities to work tirelessly with the orchestra, up to 50 hours for a single piece, to achieve such a level of understanding that the orchestral members were then free to express themselves within the concept being worked on.<br /><br />Ozawa stressed the contribution that Karajan had made to the advance of recording techniques which we all benefit from today. His choice of the Tchaikovsky was made because of the enormous identification he felt with Karajan's interpretation. Likewise with Mutter, who explained that she had played the Beethoven with Karajan more often than any other piece. Her encore was her audition piece with Karajan when she was only 13 years of age.<br /><br />The concert recorded on this disc fully fulfilled these retrospective comments. The Beethoven was given a steady but spellbinding interpretation progressing from darkness into light as Mutter explains. This, not surprisingly, was met with a prolonged ovation which was then followed by a simply sublime performance of the Bach Sarabande from the 2nd Partita. This is not a performance in the 'authentic' manner but is altogether more generous in its expression of romantic warmth. This is achieved without heaviness although it could never be accused of being speedy. In my opinion this is a performance that Karajan would have identified with and approved of.<br /><br />`Tonight this orchestra played amazingly' said Ozawa when concluding his interview held just after the concert. Both artists stressed Karajan's modesty and warmth - important recollections frequently unspoken of and therefore largely unknown.<br /><br />Ozawa was on inspirational form and his above acknowledgement of the orchestra's contribution modestly fails to fully explain why. From this viewers point of view I would suggest that they were inspired, not only by the debt that they felt towards Karajan in this series of concerts, but also by Ozawa's deeply committed, exciting and ultimately moving interpretation. Once more this is a performance that would have earned praise from Karajan himself being meticulously prepared with a strong sense of direction leading to a satisfying emotional conclusion.<br /><br />This is a remarkable memorial tribute to Karajan's 100th birthday and is given a superb recording to match with crisp, detailed and non-invasive camera work coupled with excellent sound in stereo and surround format presented in PCM 5.1 format. In my opinion this is outstanding throughout and worth every one of 5 stars.more generous in its expression of romantic warmth. 0307951529|4.0|Full disclosure:  I received a gift copy of this book from the author and as a follower of his blog, I was super excited for this book to finally be released. Make no mistake, all the examples and stories in the book are not meant to provide you with turn-key solutions.  You will already need some skills in some area (not necessarily book-learnin' skills, but any kind of skill) that people will pay for.  He tells you how to discern between two paths before striking out on your own: how you can tell if your passion will pay the bills, and how you can tell if it won't.  That's worth knowing, especially nowadays when it's all too common to drive by empty storefronts of businesses that were built on passion and enthusiasm, but had no one willing to buy from them.  What I like about the book is that there are a ton of examples of people from all walks of life and all kinds of financial backgrounds who were successful in striking out on their own.  There really are examples of attainable dreams in this tome.  Not all the examples will apply to you, and some actually sound more expensive to start than the title would have you believe, but I feel that the author probably interviewed the business owners after they had been comfortably established in their business after several years of building it.  However, don't let that turn you away from the book.  At no point does Guillebeau try to make you believe that success is going to be easy, but he's lowering the bar for entry with the $100 figure, and saying that the rest is up to your own hard work and willingness to persevere.  I like that message.  The message tells you not to be too afraid if you don't have the money to start (pretty encouraging), and gives you a little push and inspiration to turn a small business into one that can comfortably sustain you if you make some good (I didn't say all of them had to be good, sometimes you will fail) decisions along the way.  He provides realistic bounce-back tales of businesses whose lives where threatened after some horrible botch up in their early stages, only to come back again and thrive.  What I like about this book is that it's plenty accessible.  But like another reviewer, I wish it was a little heavier in providing more how-to's than on copious amounts of inspirational examples.  More how-to's would make this the perfect guide for how to get over the inertia of starting your first micro-business.

So I've had this book for 40yrs, and in all that time of adding to my library of artistic books, I've never found a better general reference for learning to draw figures (though Willy Pogany's 'Drawing Lessons' book of 1946 comes pretty close). It has excellent sections on the basics of perspective, light and shade, anatomy, proportion and composition, and the examples of Loomis's pencil craft and penmanship are superb.<br /><br />Now granted, this is an old book, entirely in black and white, stylistically mannered and from the point of view of a realist illustrator of a bygone era, rather than a of a fine artist (for whom the drawings might lack some degree of artistic expressionism or abstraction), but it is nevertheless, one of the finest books available on the subject of figurative drawing; even after all this time.<br /><br />I value it so highly, I've just bought a second copy so that when I eventually disappear, my two artistically oriented kids won't have to fight over this particular part of my library.

1451648537|4.0|This is a well written but perhaps a bit uneven book that brings us closer to the enigmatic guy in the black turtleneck we got to see  selling us the snappy new widget at Mac conventions. I must confess that during the first 1/3 of the book  I developed a visceral dislike for Jobs, his mannerisms &  narcissism that bordered on mild psychopathy. Bill Gates had him figured out from the outset when he said that there was something less than human in Jobs' tantrums.Stealing other people's ideas and selling them as his own; habitual & casual rudeness; pathological and irrational stinginess; weird hygiene; above all, the casual abandonment of people who had ceased to be useful as exemplified glaringly in the case of his daughter Lisa and her mother Crisann. Jobs was a sensitive and he used his ability to read people's emotions and desires to hurt them where it hurt most. How could one, even remotely, find a guy like this sympatico baffles me - the glib charm offensives when Jobs needed other people's money or ideas, or simply wanted to ingratiate himself, were a clear danger signal. The &#34;reality distortion field&#34; we hear about throughout the book is essentially a synonym for &#34;lying&#34; and mind control.<br /><br />Jobs' zen was more an affected show than the real thing, he clearly did not get anything at all beyond the superficial aesthetics. I was more intrigued by the strange diets, which must have kept him in a perpetual altered state. Reminded me of indigenous tribes, where &#34;shamans&#34; etc use this very method to stay on their toes in the face of danger. Some examples would be the  Jivaro (who subsist on tobacco juice), Mekeo &#34;men of sorrow&#34; who eat almost nothing to keep their 'powers', the Kogi, fasting Native Americans etc. Curious, that.<br /><br />The best thing that happened to Jobs was to be fired from Apple and to get married to a woman who would stand by his side despite the idiosyncrasies. This , one gets the sense, is where things started to shift. Suddenly he meets and has to deal with, guys with egos bigger than his own - Eisner, Katzenberg, Ellison. And pulls off, with Pixar, one of the more amazing feats in the media business. Here, unfortunately, is where the detailed psychological portrait painted by I. starts to dim and we get to hear more about the business deals; the story gets weaker the more it moves towards the present day...  in contrast to the gripping, detailed portraits of the early Apple II-Mac days, I. blithely zips through the iGadgets without letting us into the picture. I guess this may be because the real work (microprocessor chips etc) was actually created by other people whereas Jobs was fixated on the design process.<br /><br />The older Jobs gets, the more he starts to resemble a human being. He still rips people off, sells his employees' ideas as his own etc...  but his priorities start to shift. He is still unable, despite all the billions, to do a grand gesture that would come even remotely close to commitments and effort made by Buffet and Gates to better the condition of his fellow man - when it comes to money Jobs is basically a bazaar merchant. Psychologically, he is never far away from Beirut - his first impulse is always to cheat those without power to hit back and he does it just for the sake of it. Jobs is different from, say, Ellison in that he does not seem to be motivated by greed for the sake of money - his refusal to properly compensate the people who did the real work behind the glamorous exterior seems rather an obvious of psychopathology that was never resolved and disconnected the men from other human beings. Jobs was fine with the Orwellian Foxconn factories; paid minimum wage to Apple store employees who individually sold equipment worth millions while he, Steve, sat on tens of $ billions of cash in the bank. Real weirdness here.<br /><br />As he fights cancer, Jobs  starts to contemplate improving education and even chides Murdoch about the destruction that Fox &#34;news&#34; daily wreaks upon this country. That's quite an evolution for the apolitical guy who always saw himself first. But - alas - all it was, was words.<br /><br />In summary, this is a good book. At its best, the story reminded me of the Electric Koolaid Acid Test; we hear about LSD and even get to meet Dylan and Baez (Jobs' girlfriend for a time). The book slowly loses steam, as the author was clearly under some time pressure there. Yet it is well worth reading - to see how the Silicon Valley and VC operate, to see how the IP game is played, to get an insight into some of the key players in (tech) history, and to crawl under the skin of a cantankerous, selfish, intuitive, brilliant, one-of-a-kind person who created beautiful things by using his gut, and guts. He would have hated it and he would have admired it.wreaks upon this country. That's quite an evolution for the apolitical guy who always saw himself first. But - alas - all it was, was words. B00000099Y|1.0|If you went back into the late 90's/early 00's and asked me what my favorite bands were, I would have gladly answered Tool and Rage Against the Machine.  Back then, I thought both bands were such musically-brilliant, insightful bands because of the fact that they were marketed as bands that were heavy metal yet were insightful and thought-provoking, and being the gullable kid I was due to my lack of knowledge of underground music at the time, I ate them both up like hotcakes.  For the last seven years or so, I've been neck-deep in underground metal and industrial bands, and to say that I've burned my bridges to Tool and RATM would be an understatement.  Rather, it would be more appropriate to say that I hijacked a TOS-1 Buratino flamethrower tank and not only decimated said bridges with the tank's massive thermobaric rocket payload, but also decimated any nearby engineering firms that could have rebuilt those bridges to said alt-metal bands. Okay, enough of my rambling, on to reviewing Tool's \\"Aenima.\\"<br /><br />Listening to this album as an adult, I can't help but get infuriated by it.  This album infuriates me because aside from sounding so drab for being a \\"heavy\\" rock band, it totally reeks of dishonesty for the fact that it's marketed as an intelligent slab of music yet it tightly-clings to such juvenille aesthetics that don't gel at all with aesthetics that are supposed to be \\"higher class.\\"  Even if the juvenille lyrics in some songs are supposed to be ironic humor, it didn't work because I'm not laughing.<br /><br />MUSICIANS/MUSICIANSHIP<br /><br />While many of the musicians here have some instrumental talent by themselves, they don't win much points from me because they don't craft much music on this album that I actually like.<br /><br />Maynard James Keenan is an extremely overrated singer.  His vocalwork is one of the main reasons why I hate this album, since his vocals usually are either really bad \\"anguished\\" singing or pseudo-aggressive yelling, in which both styles would be right at home with your run-of-the-mill 90's nu-metal (or alternative metal for those who demand euphamisms) or Nine Inch Nails album (yuck).<br /><br />His songwriting isn't impressive at all, either.  So much of his lyrics on this album are like what you'd read from an angry high schooler's notebook who's in an introductory philosophy class, so it's like a mix of bad, angsty poetry and pseudo-intellectual ramblings.  Some songs like \\"Fourty Six and Two\\" and \\"Third Eye\\" exemplify the pseudo-intellectual lyrics, while songs like \\"Hooker With a Penis\\" (brilliant song title) have lyrics as if they were yanked straight out of an angry 15 year-old's mouth.<br /><br />Adam Jones is a guitarist that has skill (though is inferior to the likes of Chuck Schuldiner and Ron Jarzombek), but a lot of what he's made on this album is really flat and uninspired.  The only song where any of his riffs and solos sound like there's some energy in them are in \\"Third Eye,\\" while it seems like the rest are just grunge/nu-metal riffs played in an \\"artsy\\" manner or flat, simplified progressive rock riffs with an occasional polyrhythm.  Most of the solos aren't that good, either, since so many of them sound like half-baked, goofy \\"feedback\\" solos you'd hear from Tom Morello (another painfully overrated guitarist).<br /><br />Justin Chancellor's bass is interesting in areas, though nothing that really grabbed my attention.  If it counts for anything, at least his bass is more audible in this album than most rock albums out there.<br /><br />Danny Carey is a drummer that's been showered with tons of praise.  Now he is a skilled drummer, but skill with an instrument doesn't mean they automatically can make interesting songs.  True, in a lot of the songs on this album, Carey does some neat, technical stuff with his skins, but they can't save the songs for the fact that so many of them are either boring or downright irritating.<br /><br />SONGS<br /><br />The only songon this album I'd say is promising is \\"Third Eye.\\"  In fact, I'd say that for the most part, it shows that when pressed, Tool's rhythm section can come up with dynamic, interesting music (especially since this one clocks in at 14 minutes).  However, it gets ruined with Maynard's lousy vocalwork, especially when he screams out \\"Prying open my third eye!!\\".<br /><br />The \\"hit\\" songs like \\"Stinkfist\\" and \\"Aenima\\" are examples of the grunge/nu-metal songs with an \\"artsy\\" glaze applied to them, and boy do they sound annoying. They honestly don't sound much different (or much better) than the typical grunge/nu-metal gunk infesting the mainstream at the time this album came out.<br /><br />Other songs like \\"Push1t\\" and \\"Eulogy\\" are examples of trying-too-hard-yet-flat progressive-rock with the occasional \\"heavy\\" moments on this album, and when I'm not chuckling at Maynard's attempts to come off as a deep thinker, I'm usually wallowing in boredom over how bloodless most of them feel.<br /><br />There's filler tracks like \\"Message to Harry Manback,\\" \\"Intermission,\\" and \\"Die Eeir Von Satan\\" that add next to nothing in terms of musical quality, in that they only pad out the running length of the album.<br /><br />PRODUCTION<br /><br />The production quality on this album isn't bad, since all the vocals, guitars, bass, and drums come in rather clear.  However, like with all music, good sound quality can't save lousy music.<br /><br />FINAL WORD<br /><br />Despite all the heavy marketing and hype from the likes of MTV and Tool fans, this album is NOT a progressive metal masterpiece that will stretch your mind and change your life.<br /><br />If you're looking for some actual metal albums that have brilliant musicianship, innovative ideas, creativity, or thoughtful lyrics (or a combination of any), I strongly suggest you invest your time and money on these albums instead (and yes, I've listened to and own all of these):<br /><br />Death: \\"Human\\"<br />Cynic:  \\"Focus\\"<br />Pestilence:  \\"Spheres\\"<br />Phantasmagory:  \\"Odd Sounds\\"<br /> Atheist:  \\"Piece of Time\\"<br /> Spastic Ink:  \\"Ink Complete\\"<br />Blotted Science:  \\"The Machinations of Dementia\\"<br /> Gordian Knot:  \\"S/T\\"<br /> Bunkur:  \\"Bludgeon\\"<br /> Neurosis:  \\"Souls at Zero\\"<br /> Godflesh:  \\"Pure\\"<br /> Blut Aus Nord:  \\"MoRT\\"<br /> Pan.Thy.Monium:  \\"Khaooohs\\"<br /> Meshuggah:  \\"Destroy Erase Improve\\"<br /><br />Don't be surprised that if you get engrossed in any of the above albums and own a copy of Tool's \\"Aenima,\\" that you find yourself getting rid of said Tool album.on this album I'd say is promising is \\"Third Eye.\\"  In fact, I'd say that for the most part, it shows that when pressed, Tool's rhythm section can come up with dynamic, interesting music (especially since this one clocks in at 14 minutes). 1594744769|4.0|Girls who can levitate, invisible boys, a girl with the ability to summon fire in her hands or a boy that has bees living in his mouth.  These are the fantastical stories and photographs Jacob's grandfather shares with him from the time he's very young. Most people, including Jacob, think they're just that - stories illustrated by doctored and altered photographs.  But Jacob reconsiders when he finds his grandfather dying in a forest behind his house and spies the hideous monster who killed him hiding in the trees.  Jacob travels to a small island called Cairnholm where his grandfather grew up in a children's home searching for answers to his grandfather's death.  It's on this island, hidden in the bogs and swamps, that he finds information not only about his grandfather, but about himself as well.<br /><br />Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children was truly different from other teen paranormal fiction out there, and I don't want to ruin any part of it, which is why I left my synopsis so vague.  The novel is told in first person from Jacob's point of view and I have to say, being privy to Jacob's inner monologue is really entertaining.  He's snarky and intelligent and brave, and I loved seeing this world through his eyes.  I also liked the advantage of experiencing a blossoming romance through the heart of a teenage boy, since many of the paranormal books tell romantic stories from a girl's point of view.  It was refreshing to get a guy's perspective on love for a change.<br /><br />What also made the book really intriguing and unique was the inclusion of actual authentic vintage photographs.  There are pictures strewn throughout of photos with a very circus-like feel of a boy lifting an enormous boulder, a man with a mouth on the back of his head, a contortionist, and a boy with the body of a dog.  I was absolutely amazed that these came from actual collectors who had found many of the photographs from garage and antique sales, and are, with the exception of some minor enhancements, undoctored.  They gave a very unique flavor to the book, and we are allowed to experience the same conflict as the main character as to whether or not we can believe the photographs as evidence of his grandfather's stories.<br /><br />Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children has a little bit of something for everyone - mystery, romance, adventure, action - all with a unique storyline and perspective.  It's worth delving into if you need to spice up your reading list with something out of the ordinary.<br /><br />from blog Adults Reading YA on Blogger

B007V508CC|3.0|The irony of titling their new album \\"Overexposed\\" must have been lost on Adam Levine and Maroon 5. The album is overproduced, over-compressed and over-just-about-everything. Like so many bands in the current pop zeitgeist, they decided that they needed to conform to current popular production memes, which means having a guest rapper-of-the-moment (Wiz Khalifa, adding bonus profanities to \\"Payphone\\"), auto-tune, writer/producers of the now (Max Martin, Ryan Tedder) and multiple other steps that rob the band of any of their previous individual qualities. Take Adam out of the equation, and this would be another generic, over-compressed/produced pop-album.<br /><br />The saving grace is Adam, who's white-boy soul is on a par with Daryl Hall at this point. Given the material he has to work with, he milks every hook and croon he can. Good numbers like \\"Lucky Strike,\\" \\"Lady Killer\\" and even the obscenity laced \\"Payphone\\" stick like rubber cement. I'll make a quick point to say that I'm not a prude, but for a band that seemingly prides itself on their pop craftsmanship, the swearing in \\"Payphone\\" and \\"Tickets\\" just seems more gratuitous than effective, like the band has to prove how hard they are. But when you're capable of making really good light funk that hits its groove like \\"Fortune Teller\\" and \\"Doin' Dirt,\\" you don't need to prove you're anything but a great pop band.<br /><br />Which is what, ultimately, Maroon 5 is. \\"Overexposed\\" hits all its marks seemingly without effort, with craftsmanship that most any other band would sell their bubblegum machines for. Adam's unique voice gives the band enough identity to cut through the generic sounding production here, and when they horn-pump Prince's slinky \\"Kiss\\" (a bonus track on the deluxe version), they demonstrate that they're above the cookie-cutter sound that plagues this album.

B007VTVGAQ|5.0|If you LOVED Extraordinary Machine and consider it one of the best albums in recent memory, you might want to skip this record. This record has no polish, no hip-hop beats, no fun/silly/dancy hooks, and almost nothing light-hearted. Almost.<br /><br />Alternatively, if you're a fan of Janis Joplin, Animal Collective, and more complicated - and yes, more difficult - art, then this is your dream come true.<br /><br />I fall in the latter camp.<br /><br />This album is WAY more art than luster. It's more honest that way.<br /><br />Upon the first listen, I was slightly put off by the transition between what one might initially consider \\"cute\\" and \\"interesting\\" in the opening track \\"Every Single Night\\" (which recalls 2005's \\"Extraordinary Machine\\") and \\"Daredevil,\\" a song which seems totally unpredictable and wild by contrast. The percussion, including the percussive piano chords, are driving and maddening! Her vocal performance is top-notch, and when she gets to the jazzy, Janis-esque bridge, you're having a \\"wow\\" moment. It may take a few spins, this isn't the kind of song (or album!) that's immediately gratifying for everybody. This song is indicative of the album as a whole in that it's challenging, rewarding, off-putting, and gripping all at the same time.<br /><br />\\"Valentine\\" is a welcome respite from the madness of the second track, reminiscent of Fiona's earlier albums, especially When The Pawn.... The stand-up bass is positively delicious. She injects some humor and sweetness into a rather melancholy song. It feels like an instant classic.<br /><br />The next song \\"Jonathan\\" brings us back into challenging territory. It begins with extremely unpleasant sounds that remind me of Nine Inch Nails. Very industrial, mechanical sounds. It's a meandering piece, and the conflict of the relationship is stunningly apparent in the music, in the lyrics, in the vocals. It's actually quite a feat. I've heard complaints that this song runs too long, which is fair, but beingthat this song is about an awkward, bumpy train ride, lasting too long in discomfort is kind of the point.<br /><br />I had to revisit the next track, \\"Left Alone,\\" a few times to believe it. In fact, I still can't believe it. It's an unambiguously ugly, mean, self-loathing, self-pitying cluster-mess! She verges on so much musical potential - you think for a moment she might take you there, to some place soaring and heavenly. But her voice taunts. It contorts. She starts groaning and squealing sometimes in the same word. It's very jarring. Again Fiona has put a very uncomfortable emotion into songform. Absolutely brilliant. Has to be heard to be believed. Multiple times. \\"My ills are reticulate, my woes are granular/The ants weigh more than the eleph-ants.\\" No one is making music like this nowadays folks, NO ONE.<br /><br />\\"Werewolf\\" is the \\"Paper Bag\\" (from 1999's When The Pawn...) of The Idler Wheel.... It's plaintive and pretty. Rare on this record. It's reminiscent of older music, Kurt Weill? Carole King? Probably not. The song is gorgeous heard out of the context of the album, but it shines particularly brightly among the other songs. It's so gentle, so humble. It's another respite after the density and difficulty of the last song. A good thing too, because the next song, \\"Periphery\\" is not an easy listen. Even in a simple and gorgeous tune, Fiona manages to have some fun: at one point, a banjo makes a cameo for 4 notes, and then bows out. Another fantastic detail to this song is the sample from the playground. It somehow captures nostalgia, innocence, sadness, happiness and terror all together. I love her spontaneity on this record, it is beyond refreshing. \\"Nothing wrong when/a song ends in a minor key.\\" You can say that again, Fi. Your entire album is in a minor key.<br /><br />\\"Periphery\\" is discordant and unpleasant. Deliberately. It opens up with ripping sounds. Again, Apple hints at harmony once in a while, with backup vocals popping up right in the middle of the song for a few bars, sounding almost - almost! - harmonious. But then the piano goes bonkers and she sings \\"I don't appreciate/people who/don't appreciate...You let me down/I don't even like you anymore at all!\\" Absolutely bizarrely delicious. And then it sounds like she's ripping up something again. I heard a pillow was one of the instruments in the liner notes, and this could easily be a pillow getting shredded and it's easy to imagine feathers floating around when the song ends.<br /><br />The next song snuck up on me. It's a real stunner. \\"Regret\\" starts out with mechanical beats, like an unhappy machine with missing pieces. Again: immediate tension. The piano is also displaying a longing: chord. pause. chord. pause. chord. pause. After being so off-kilter in \\"Periphery,\\" \\"Regret\\" feels measured and sure-footed, even when she sings the ridiculous/hilarious highlight lyric: \\"I ran out of white doves' feathers/to soak up the hot piss that comes from your mouth/every time you address me.\\"<br /><br />But finally, it's on \\"Anything We Want\\" that Fiona finally reveals her secret garden. The toys are all in full effect, bangs and taps and clings and clangs, with Fiona's grounding piano leading the way. In the middle there's a sort-of crescendo chord change that seems to finally peel back the ivies and let the sun shine in on you (and her). It's a unique and wonderful listening experience.<br /><br />The album closer, \\"Hot Knife\\" is indeed hot and sharp, a perfect upbeat finish that helps alleviate some of the emotional exhaustion one may feel at the end of such laundry airing. It's infectious and warm and yummy the way \\"The First Taste\\" (on 1996's Tidal) was, except in a much more nuanced execution. \\"You can relax around me.\\"<br /><br />It's easy to think The Idler Wheel... is almost under-produced, until you realize that the vocals are well-layered and the sampling is perfect - and then you realize that these complex and difficult emotions are perfectly framed by this production. It's a strange sound taken as a whole, but a sound Fiona is comfortable with. Not that she didn't previously have full artistic control over her music, but she was kind of wandering around looking for a sound on her first 3 records - and that was a beautiful and wonderful journey to follow her on - but THIS is how Fiona Apple wants to sound. This is an artist at her peak, honestly showcasing her talents, her toys, her thoughts, her turmoil, her artwork, her ideas.<br /><br />And it's astounding, nothing less.
B007I2BZIE|4.0|First, I want to point out that the 4 stars I give this album represent my view of its standing in comparison to other Rush albums, not how good I think it is in general.  Since, in 40+ years Rush hasn't put out a bad album, or even a bad song, the 4 stars here would surpass mostg other album's 5 stars.  I would also like to point out that I am not a rabid Rush fan.  If you ARE a rabid, or perhaps even a moderately afflicted, Rush fan, you may not, initially, be suitably impressed.  You mostly likely will not feel let down, but you may not be inclined to sing its praises, either.  BUT, give it time.  The reason a rabid Rush fan may not fully embrace the album initially is the same reason non Rush fans, or non rabid Rush fans will enjoy it more than other Rush albums:  It sounds more basic than anything Rush has previously done, although that's kind of like saying the new Ferrari isn't as fast as the previous version.  On this album, the songs are not of epic length, the time changes and polyphonic rhythms of Neil's drumming, the pedal work of Alex's playing and the frenetic lead-like bass playing of Geddy are not so in your face as earlier Rush albums.  So, if you give this ablum a once-through or a casual listen, you may find it less inspired than what you would expect from Rush.  If that's the case, don't stop there. Listen again and again. Each listen reveals a greater complexity, a greater maturity, than the last listen.  Once you do that, you will realize it is infused with all the elements that make Rush great. B0079XPUOW|5.0|This book comes in 4 parts: the novel(la) RedShirts, and the 3 codas.<br /><br />Redshirts is sci-fi comedy in the style of Terry Pratchett and Douglas Adams: Sardonic dialogue, zany situations and cleverly written characters pull a ridiculous plot together in a way that's genius rather than camp. Redshirts is insightful comedy, heartstring-tugging tragedy and some pretty awesome action scenes all wrapped up in an obvious love of the sci-fi genre. By itself, the novel is worth the 12 bucks.<br /><br />The codas, however, are what blew my mind.  The first coda continues the tongue in cheek tone of the novel, but contrasts that with a deep sense of worry.  The tone of the 2nd two codas are not humourous.  They are decidely somber and touching.  They left me with questions about my own place in the multiverse.<br /><br />Ultimately, the true question of any novel is \\"Would you recommend it to a friend?\\"  Yes, I already have.  Four times.  If you love sci-fi, or humor, or philosophical dilemmas or bad acting or great writing or finishing a book and immediately wanting to re-read it, BUY THIS ONE!

B007WFQZI2|5.0|If you bother to trace my product reviews, they are mostly centered around music-related topics. However, my preference for the analog format is rooted in my long-time obsession with music and especially hard rock and metal. In the late '80s I was a long haired thrasher, and I was introduced to Kreator and other German groups such as Sodom, Assassin, and Destruction through friends and metal magazines. Among my first purchases included \\"Terrible Certainty\\" and Extreme Aggression\\" upon the latter's release in 1989. At that time most of my collection was on CD or cassette as I used my roommate's turntable for the limited vinyl in my collection. I made mix tapes constantly so my Walkman would keep me isolated, and also for the stereo at work. Boy did the waitresses hate my music, they were in fear of me too, sporting \\"Metal Up Your Ass\\" t-shirts and such. Anyhow, Kreator stood out for Mille's coarse vocals, and the sometimes too fast to stay tight tunes. Nevertheless, like many groups at the time (Sacred Reich amongst others), they 'grew' into their musical abilities and in time they became masters of a style that was, until recently, on the fringe of society-- accused of being Satanic, violent, misogynistic, etc... (like rap almost...). To this day their covers follow the devilish imagery of old... but<br /><br />This new album, literally, on vinyl, truly is exceptional. I have it on CD too, pre-ordered it on Amazon, and it struck my fancy immediately with its maturity, at least in the melodic sense, but Kreator is not getting soft with age either. Indeed, they are still kicking butt, and I plan to see them on tour with Accept in LA this coming September. Now for the vinyl review: Because my ears were accustomed to the CD 'tone' if you will, I immediately noticed the fuller sound in all frequencies, top to bottom, and especially the bottom. Furthermore, Mille's vocals are better presented in the mix- center and audible, even at very high volume (as you know already, the only way to listen to thrash). I played both dics (by the way, they are red vinyl with black spatters, not yellow as depicted in the product image. The sleeves are high quality paper, but I still recommend a good wash and using Mofi anti-static sleeves afterward to preserve the originals. Washing also removes the inherent static that nearly all new and used LPs arrive with. I think the only thing that sucks is having to get up and flip the records, but the superior sound is worth the extra output of energy. I think you owe it to yourself to get acquainted with how much better sounds on vinyl. While my original collection is scattered to the winds, I can rebuild it with vinyl versions of the old CDs (whenever possible) and collect new LPs too. Look for the release programs by Back On Black from the UK who reissue 180 gram pressings of legendary hard rock by Motorhead, Dark Angel, UFO, THin Lizzy, and many other gems. In the meantime, I will play this Kreator slab of heavy vinyl for days and weeks to come- naturally, I will take care of my investment. Rock on!!he only way to listen to thrash). B0087YYHZU|5.0|I should probably start this review by admitting that I'm not really a fan of the old-fashioned movie musical.  (There, I said it; now I just gotta hope that my dance school lets me finish out my lessons.) Films like 'Oklahoma,' 'An American in Paris,' and 'Seven Brides for Seven Brothers' just don't cut my mustard.<br /><br />To be fair, there have been exceptions to this (`The Wizard of Oz,' `Mary Poppins,' 'My Fair Lady,' `Fiddler on the Roof,' and `Singin' in the Rain'), but as the saying goes, these tend to prove my rule.  Each of the aforementioned exceptions has some ineffable quality besides their nice tunes that distinguishes them as cinematic gems - an infectious energy, an epic scope, or an emphasis on story and character that I'd always felt evaded the standard old-Hollywood musical.<br /><br />`Singin' in the Rain' works so well for me, I think, because it's actually about something beyond its admittedly great musical numbers. It locks onto a very specific moment in film history - the transition from silents to `talkies' - and miraculously develops a very funny, fully realized story with genuine character development that still allows for some wonderful song and dance numbers. (As an added bonus, these numbers usually seem to grow organically out of the plot. Except, perhaps, for the scene with Kelly and Cyd Charisse, but I digress...)  And those dance numbers *are* wonderfully done, and are often astonishingly choreographed.  How Donald O'Connor wasn't carried off set on a stretcher after the final take of `Make `em Laugh' I will never know, but his work led to lead to one of the (if not *the*) greatest movie musical scenes ever filmed.<br /><br />The film is rightly considered a classic, and one of the best films of all time.  But more than that, it is quite simply one of the most fun.<br /><br />*Please Note: at the time of this review's publication, the Blu Ray hasn't yet been released. 1612680011|5.0|Rich Dad Poor Dad by Robert Kiyosaki is a highly successful book that criticizes the status quo and calls for a change in how we organize our lives. It calls for us to challenge some things that our parents have taught us concerning success. In the book, the author shows us the old school teachings of the poor dad and the innovative thinking of the rich dad who succeeded even without following the conventional path.<br /><br />If you are deeply steeped in the old school thinking of `go to college, study hard and get a good job,' this book may challenge you. In the ever-changing economy, this kind of thinking is becoming obsolete as we see more and more people become successful in business without even having completed college. The book is not cancelling out the need for school; it is proposing that education does not guarantee success in life unless you are able to think innovatively and competitively. Today, there are no guarantees that a large company will hire you just because you worked hard in school.<br /><br />This is a recommended read for teachers, parents, students and anyone who may be looking for an alternative way of accumulating wealth other than the conventional, rote approach.

B000002J1Y|4.0|This is an ambitious live set with some impressive artwork by Roger Dean (I remember the quadruple gatefold sleeve and opening it up and not knowing how to fold it back properly, I nearly ruined a friend's older brother's LP's inside- sorry for that!). Aside from that, it's Alan White's debut with Yes (he's one of the few members of Yes, whom though not an original member, stayed in Yes after he joined as long as the Yes band name existed). Still, Bill Bruford plays on 3 songs (Long Distance Runaround, The Fish and Perpetual Change), as he left the band in the middle of their 1972 Close To The Edge tour. The album features exclusively songs from their Yes Album, Fragile and Close To The Edge albums. It all begins with a recorded version of the finale of Stravinsky's The Firebird Suite (if you'll pardon the digression, I find it hard not to think of Yes whenever I hear this- I often think Jon Anderson's voice would've been appropriate at the finale of Fantasia 2000). Then after the final bars sound, Yes rips into Siberian Khatru. Rick Wakeman plays his harpsichord piece on a Hammond organ (not bad, just different). This would be the opening track for many of their tours. Following, you have Heart Of The Sunrise (Alan plays a tempo faster than the original). Going back to the earlier moments of the tour, Bill takes the drumkit and includes an impressive drum solo on Perpetual Change. And You And I features a different intro from the album and Steve Howe's echoing feedback at the end is more pronounced than on the studio version. Steve plays Mood For A Day on a steel string acoustic and the appreciative crowd is faded out. Later, we fade back in as Jon sings a few bars of the finale of Stravinsky's The Rite Of Spring before he introduces Rick on keyboards. Rick plays selections of the then-upcoming The Six Wives Of Henry VIII (featuring a few of the movements in addition to some old school melodrama which would make Charlie Chaplin proud, the Hallelujah Chorus and a finale of some synthesized fireworks). Steve introduces the opening riff of Roundabout on electric guitar. Jon sings the chorus slightly differently and Chris and Steve join in after Steve's opening guitar solo is reprised. The coda ends more rocking and Jon says goodnight to the audience. I've Seen All Good People features some acoustic guitar work from Steve (the \\"Your Move\\" sequence) and the ending differs with some singalong chorus at the end of \\"All Good People.\\" Then, Rick and Steve play their parts on the intro of Long Distance Runaround, at a slightly faster tempo. Steve plays the bridge between that song and \\"The Fish,\\" Chris's bass playing tour de force, in which he plays most of the multi-tracked parts individually. Close To The Edge features the pre-recorded birds and Steve's guitar solo at the beginning differs due to probably not getting the same pedals. Also, the finale which on the record has the Bb, Gm, F, Dm structure is changed to Ab, Fm, Eb, Cm so Jon can more easily hit the high notes. Yours Is No Disgrace gets 4 extra minutes due to some jamming. Rick adds some impressive piano at the beginning before the majestic opening bars and Steve plays some fast and furious riffs before Rick plays the organ leading to \\"Deaf-defying, mutilating...\\" Jon for some reason starts out in Dm before going into the key of D. Appropriately, the set closes with Starship Trooper. Jon's voice cracks a tad at the line \\"Shining wings.\\" Steve substitutes the acoustic guitar of the Disillusion segment with electric guitar and the band breaks before singing \\"Folloooooooow.\\" Finally, at the Wurm segment, Steve jams away at the G/Eb/C segment, with some fine improvisation from Rick on keyboards. Jon closes with a high B note and then thanks the audience and bids them goodnight. Though the recording of this may sound a bit sloppy (I have heard some CDs have improved on the sound quality), this album features some very spirited performances from Jon Anderson, Steve Howe, Chris Squire, Rick Wakeman, Alan White and Bill Bruford.

0765348276|5.0|I've been reading John Scalzi's blog, Whatever, for a few years now, but I haven't gotten around to reading one of his books until now. I think Old Man's War is one of his most famous books, and I was able to get it for a bargain at Half Price Books, so that's the one I chose to start with.<br /><br />From reading his blog, I expected Scalzi to be a good writer, but he exceeded my expectations by far! Old Man's War is set in a future where humans are colonising the stars, but livable space is at a minimum, so there are constant battles. The Colonial Union, an entity entirely separate from Earth's government, controls all human space efforts. The only way to get off-world is either to get picked by them to colonise, or join the Colonial Defense Forces (CDF) on your 75th birthday. John Perry is doing the latter.<br /><br />The world of Old Man's War is fascinating, although it seems like it's about to come crumbling down. Given the mystery of the CDF, I expected a grand conspiracy or a secret plot, but Scalzi plays it totally straight. The book was obviously inspired by Starship Troopers (which I actually like a lot), but has better writing and an author that's not crazy. I haven't read a good adventure story in a while, and this book definitely filled a craving I didn't even know I had.<br /><br />I loved the idea of the CDF recruiting old people to fight, since they have the life experience necessary to be wise and understand the importance of their fight. It's a very sensible idea. John Perry is a great protagonist, and every character in the book, no matter how minor, is full of life. Scalzi has a great sense of humour, and it shows!<br /><br />I can't wait to read the next book, [[ASIN:0765354063 The Ghost Brigades]].

B0053KWDES|5.0|I purchased the HiFiMan RE262, and love it! I found it to be pretty much perfect driven by my 6.1 Receiver, and pretty good on my FiiO E6 despite the obvious power limitations. For those of you that don't know, the RE262 is a 150 Ohm IEM. This means that it takes considerable drive from an amp to perform at its best. The FiiO E6 just didn't feel powerful enough for it, although it had been more than sufficient for my other low-impedance earphones. Also the RE262 is a mid-to-high frequency emphasizing IEM. The E6 could boost the bass, but it sounded like it had been modified and kind of trashed.<br /><br />I knew I needed more power. After some shopping around at the sub-$100 portable amp level, I decided on the E11 as it was supposed to have a better amp than the E7 DAC/Amp and I didn't need the DAC, so it was pretty simple. Honestly there are not many competitive amps outside of FiiO when it comes to portable amps under $100.<br /><br />I have to say, I'm very impressed with the E11. It manages to power the RE262, and it is probably one of the most hungry portable audio devices you'll have to drive. Not many portables go higher than 150 Ohms. More impressive though, in my opinion, is the equalizer/bass boost feature. While the E6 could increase the volume of the bass, the E11 manages to preserve the tight nature of the bass, increasing volume while remaining clean. I can feel the rumble now. That's just something that the RE262 just isn't known for, either. I'm not a bass head by any means, but the bass boost is surely powerful and clean enough to make the tamest low end sound tight, and give it *plenty* more body.<br /><br />If you need more power on the go, try this amp out. I doubt you will be disappointed.<br /><br />Note: If you would like some more headroom and dynamic range, I suggest you give your low-impedance headphones the gift of an in line resistor and boost them. You should be able to find a quality resistor adapter for around $12 on the big auction site that everyone knows. The resistors are cheap enough that you should be able to get a few in 75, 150, and 225 or 300 Ohms.

0385335997|5.0|After the near-pointless 600-page flashback that was [[ASIN:0385335970 Dragonfly in Amber (Outlander, Book 2)]], I was reluctant to start Voyager. But like the whispers and hums through the stones of Craigh na Dun, it pulled me back in.<br /><br />It's a good quarter of the novel before Clarie returns to 18th-century Scotland, but a lot happens before that point. We get a quick glimpse of Jamie's return to Lallybroch after the Battle of Culloden and his subsequent imprisoning at Ardsmuir prison. There he meets Lord John Grey, who is another character out of time--gay in the 18th-century English military. I'm glad he turned out to be an honorable man, and a good friend to Jamie, unlike the other despicable gay characters we've met during the course of this series.<br /><br />Anyway, we also see Claire in the 60s, trying to figure out what happened to Jamie by pouring over historical documents. Gabaldon is good at tying things together like this. Claire's deceased husband, Frank, was a researcher, and the skills Clarie picked up during their troubled marriage come to good use.<br /><br />One thing I really appreciated Gabaldon doing is making Frank a sympathetic character. It would have been easy for him to just leave Claire, but he tried to love her, even though she couldn't love him back.<br /><br />The narrative returns to its usual first-person POV once Claire travels back through the stones. Ooh boy, are things really rolling in the 18th-century. Her and Jamie's journey takes them all over the place, way beyond Scotland and France, in pursuit of treasure and pirates. These are real pirates of the Caribbean, and it's fascinating to see how they lived. Never before has reading a book made me seasick.<br /><br />When magic rears its head in this series, it's always riveting and shocking, and a bizarre ritual at the end of Voyager is one of the strangest, most vivid things I've ever read. I had to go back and re-read it to make sure I hadn't just dreamed it the next day.<br /><br />I am normally averse to long books because with my short attention span, my eyes wander to my TBR pile. What's next, what's next? But with Voyager, I wanted to go back and read it again from the beginning. That is exceptionally rare, and I will treasure this book for a long time. 1469984202|1.0|The reviews are overwhelmingly positive about his work, but I had trouble getting through much. I did finish it, but considered it a PWOT. The simplistic writing and continual dependence on emotional string tugging instead of story development was bad enough, but the implausibility of the silo environment and all the myriad points of just wrong science is the killer. I am a scientist by training and know many things about this kind of autarky and closed loop environment, especially heat transfer, air exchange, and hydroponics.  What was used as &#34;normal&#34; is totally unlikely in this story, especially as it alludes to hundreds of years in existence. Nothing is going to function for so long, even with frequent repairs and tons of spares. The tech level is clearly not sustainable without a corresponding mountain of spare parts for all the advanced machinery and consumables. For example, looking at the data processing capabilities and the grow lights depicted in this would not mean much to most. However, grow lights certainly do not last centuries, and even considering a minor miracle such as microwave powered calcium/sulfur dust lamps providing photosynthetic active radiation, the microwave magnetron would be junk after a decade. CPUs, DRAM, and hard drives are flaky even now, not to even consider centuries of use and repair. Current grow lamp technology such as sodium vapor and metal halide lamps would last a few years at most. LEDs are even shorter lived.  In addition, much is made of crops such as oats and fruit juice grown under hydroponics; that is simply not even remotely probable due to space limitations and the carbon fixing nature of the species used and maximum yield per unit of energy limitations, even imagining outlandish bio-engineering for productivity. The energy does not balance at all, and without incredible heat sinks, everyone in an enclosed silo would bake in short order, especially considering the apparently highly toxic outside air and the obvious lack of filtration to remove contaminants/absorb high energy radiation from particles.  If you didn't know, the huge Mall of the Americas in frigid Minneapolis is heated solely by the activity of electrical fixtures and the inhabitants walking around shopping.Imagine the energy using and  squandering depicted in this story and you realize with a tiny bit of physics and thermodynamics that it simply doesn't work out. This is not a science fiction novel; it may be a romantic relationship tale and if poorly cataloged by Amazon, then I apologize for being a pedant. I do not apologize for the infantile writing and boring nature of the story. Even if one ignores the technical impossibilities, the lame unskilled writing and boring nature of the story would not justify spending even a penny on this tripe.  UPDATE - Any moron reading this is obviously not graduating grammar school. This is terrible writing.

0062206281|5.0|At once a sequel to The Angel's Game and both a sequel and prequel to The Shadow of the Wind, The Prisoner of Heaven continues to tie together the twisting and melancholy narratives of David Mart&iacute;n and Daniel Sempere. This time, Zaf&oacute;n uses the unlikeliest of sources to accomplish this intertwining of narratives: at the heart of The Prisoner of Heaven, though not the titular prisoner itself, is Ferm&iacute;n Romero de Torres, and his story, from his first steps through the gates at Castle Montju√Øc, to his eventual meeting with Daniel Sempere, is surprisingly heartbreaking and and important to the overall narrative of the series' overall story.<br /><br />In The Shadow of the Wind, Ferm&iacute;n's role as sidekick often fell into the role of comedic relief, allowing Zaf&oacute;n to break the tension with a well-timed joke about flatulence or an off-beat observation about the world, or those who people it. His place in the novel is important for helping to maintain tone, and showcasing Zaf&oacute;n's sly humour, but the reader always had the sense the Ferm&iacute;m's past held secrets as dark and interesting as anyone else in the novel. The Prisoner of Heaven explores some of those secrets, but not all. In response to Ferm&iacute;n's upcoming nuptiuals, Daniel sets out to ensure that his friend can be legally wed under his nom de plume, Ferm&iacute;n Romero de Torres, and in doing so discovers secrets about his own past and his connection with David Mart&iacute;n, lightly touched upon in The Angel's Game. It's a thrilling ride for anyone who has closely followed the labyrinth of relationships that Zaf&oacute;n has woven through his novels.<br /><br />One aspect that surprised me is how The Prisoner of Heaven makes The Angel's Game a stronger novel, by exploring David Mart&iacute;n from the perspective of an outsider. It's difficult to go into without digging deep into spoiler territory, which is not my objective for this review, but it sheds a lot of light on Mart&iacute;n's actions in The Angel's Game and explicitly explains the origins of his delusions, suggesting that the confusing narrative of The Angel's Game, often considered one of its flaws when considering it as a stand-alone novel, might serve a greater role in the series as a whole. I have one prediction for the final novel that, if it comes true, will be incredibly bold and perfectly executed by Zaf&oacute;n. Time will tell if I'm correct, but it will make subsequent re-reads of the entire series take on a new perspective.<br /><br />There are the familiar characters that we all met and grew to love in The Shadow of the Wind. Ferm&iacute;n, of course, plays an important role, as does Daniel, though his next time to truly shine will be in the following novel, but it was pleasent and nostalgic to again be re-introduced to Bea and Daniel's father, and Issac at the Cemetery of Forgotten Books, all of whom have grown up a bit, matured and evolved since we last saw them. Except Isaac; he never seems to change. And that's just fine.<br /><br />Barcelona, too, has changed since readers last visited, slowly crawling out from under the shadow of World War II and the civil war that wracked much of Spain through the latter-half of the '30s. Gone is the baroque and ethereal Barcelona, ripe for the lingering ghosts and haunted dreams that formed much of the core tale in The Shadow of the Wind, replaced with a more contemporary Barcelona, snow falling over the city, instead of sunshine bleaching its streets. Due to the dual-timeline structure of the narrative, Zaf&oacute;n is able to press these two visions of Barcelona together, illustrating the cities transformation through the intervening years between the `beginning' of Ferm&iacute;n's tale (1939) to the opening pages of this tale (1957). It's another touching love letter from Zaf&oacute;n to the City of Counts.<br /><br />The Prisoner of Heaven is a worthy follow-up to The Shadow of the Wind and everything I hoped The Angel's Game would be. It is not as deep or labyrinthine as The Shadow of the Wind, by virtue of its length, and it does not play tricks on the reader through a twisted, unreliable narrator like The Angel's Game (or does it?), but stands between the two of them as a strong novel that, while it doesn't stand entirely on its own, as the previous two novels did, appears to be the keystone novel in Zaf&oacute;n's series. Many questions are raised in The Prisoner of Heaven, pondering the relationship between the tales of Daniel Sempere and David Mart&iacute;n, and the final volume of the quartet, along with those answers, cannot come soon enough. The Prisoner of Heaven is a near pitch-perfect novel, and fans of The Shadow of the Wind have much to look forward to.ength, and it does not play tricks on the reader through a twisted, unreliable narrator like The Angel's Game (or does it?), but stands between the two of them as a strong novel that, while it doesn't stand entirely on its own, as the previous two novels did, appears to be the keystone novel in Zaf√≥n's series. B000002HH9|3.0|Over the years this album has received a lot of flack. Overall, it's underrated despite its shortcomings. The music scene had changed in four years, the time between \\"Wish\\" and \\"Wild Mood Swings\\", as did the Cure. The line up was in limbo, as Smith had just recovered from a legal battle with former member Lol Tolhurst. Grunge had eaten its way through the mainstream conscience, and subsequently killed off many of the great acts of the previous decade.....or significantly decreased their relevance in the then new musical landscape. This was already the Post Grunge era, 1996. Siouxsie and the Banshees disbanded, but ironically the Sex Pistols reunited. And in the midst of all this the Cure were about to defy the odds and not go under after losing key members Porl Thompson and Boris Williams....Hence the birth of \\"Wild Mood Swings\\"; The controversial rebirth of the Cure.<br /><br />  The first misconception of \\"WMS\\" is that it is a happy album; It is not entirely. Like many Cure releases it is eclectic in mood, hence the title. \\"Want\\" is a very epic and dark opener, and one of the best Cure songs ever written. With this intro you would think The Cure had reawakened like a sleeping giant until the pointless misfire of \\"Club America\\" so soon leads the album astray. And then \\"This Is A Lie\\" makes a remarkable recovery and gets the album back on track. \\"The 13th\\" is a silly affair, but I still find it an intriguing and fun track. The Latin flavor is seldom used in the Cure, and the last time I can recall that spice was \\"The Blood\\" from 1985's \\"Head On The Door\\".<br /><br />  \\"Strange Attraction\\" is their poppiest moment, both lyrically clever and colorful. \\"Mint Car\\" begins to tip the scale in terms of the \\"happy\\" element because it is so blatant. This is where those misconceptions start. I also always disagreed this song being released as a single. It's not terrible by any means, but it's also not challenging like their previous pop outings such as \\"Lovecats\\" and \\"Close To Me\\". The somber \\"Jupiter Crash\\" takes the record on another detour, followed by another course correction, \\"Round & Round & Round\\", lyrics that remind me of the club life social rut and its shallow tendencies. \\"Gone\\" drags its feet out of bed, but is somehow playful and endearing.<br /><br />  \\"Numb\\" is thoughtful yet dreary, but by no means a bad song either. Again the erratic \\"WMS\\" rollercoaster spirals into yet another loop as \\"Return\\" picks it back up, but it's not until \\"Trap\\" that Robert snaps into an angst not heard since the beginning of the album, and it is a fine Rock number indeed. \\"Treasure\\" is a beautiful and bittersweet come down as \\"WMS\\" is about to close shop with the final track \\"Bare\\". At this point the listener is left exhausted by all the twists, turns, and detours laid down on its course, and I'd say at moments nearing derailment. The closer (\\"Bare\\") is not one of the strongest Cure songs. It almost feels like Robert and company are straining to find any real ounce of emotion left as they are running out of steam....The melancholy words and such seem almost contrived. The album's conclusion is anti-climatic. Although variety is always welcomed, the album lacks focus. It's also not as adventurous as the delirious \\"Top\\" album despite the common thread of both being mixed bags.<br /><br />  \\"WMS\\" is a departure from any previous era of the band. The album was recorded in a house as opposed to a regular studio. This situation introduced an intimacy that is yet to be found before or after this release. The recording quality and production is unique for a Cure album, I'll admit. The use of horns and orchestral arrangements are reminiscent of \\"Kiss Me Kiss Kiss Me\\", yet both characteristics still lend a distinctiveness of their own to this album. If anything, the use of these elements, only add to the degree of intimacy that helps define \\"WMS\\". It's one of the album's strengths, intentional or not. It's almost enough to counteract some of the weakersongs.<br /><br />  Many uncertainties within the band seem to have been the backdrop of the making of this record. It was the longest gap between Cure albums. When the skies were finally clear again for the group something different took shape. This is drummer Jason Cooper's debut, and the return of keyboardist Roger O'Donnell who had not been present in the line up since \\"Disintegration\\". \\"WMS\\" marks the beginning of not only a new line up for the Cure, but a new era. The Cure had returned, however taken a slight fall from the success of \\"Wish\\". \\"WMS\\" is nowhere near a perfect Cure album, but it is probably their most misunderstood body of work. Given the circumstances surrounding the album, I'd say they could've done much much worse. When the epitaph for the band is written, \\"WMS\\" should be remembered  as a transitional marquee for the group; One that was struggling to find its place again in a music scene that is constantly changing, erratic and unpredictable.......Much like the record itself.
