0307352145|5.0|This is a very well researched and well written book. It's got great anecdotes about people who are introverts and also shy people. It includes lots of research from psychology, sociology and other social and medical sciences about introversion and associated personality types such as shyness, sensitivity and so forth.<br /><br />The author has a very good grasp of the many aspects of introversion from a scientific point of view (draws on a huge body of studies) and also a more personal perspective too. Cain has suffered through societies marginalization of introverts because she herself is introverted and because she is also extremely observant (a trait common in introverts) her insights into the experiences of people like her are highly valuable. Her understanding of the research is impeccable as far as I could tell (which is a testament to her skills, intelligence and care as a researcher because her formal area of expertise is the law, not social science).<br /><br />The greatest contribution I think this thorough book will do for introverts will be the acceptance from introverts of their own personality trait and see it as a value instead of a burden. Society will also benefit immensely from acceptance in seeing introverts not as having a disorder and in need to be treated but as people that are part of the beautiful mosaic that make up human personalities. By ignoring introverts or marginalizing them, society does itself a huge disfavor because introverts are some of the greatest human capital we have with their creativity, open-mindedness, sensitivity and compassion. She painstakingly describes the areas in which introverts have made massive positive impacts on society and also describes many salient instances of how when their voices are drowned out by the din of extrovert-biased society, significant detriment to the world results (the recent global financial collapse is one such example).<br /><br />I think this book has a good chance to be a paradigm changer in how society treats introverts. It took 5 years to write it but I think readers will clearly see the effort, care and thoroughness Cain has invested. The rest of the world need to stop blabbering and be silent for a moment and listen to introverts. They can start by reading this book.<br /><br />However, just a quick note: I have the kindle version and unfortunately, most of the notes (and citations) have not been linked to the body of the text. There's a note section in the back but I cannot get to it from the body of the text because the hypertext links has not been added. This is a problem with kindle, however, not with the content of the book and I hope kindle will fix this problem ASAP.
B00846REIS|4.0|After just finishing Bared to You, I found myself jonesing to write down my thoughts on the \\"provocative\\" read.  Typically drawn to more academic books/classics, I decided to spice up my summer with jumping on the 50 Shades bandwagon and read the whole trilogy.  And Bared to You, from the characters to the cover, is obviously a 50 Shades piggy back ride.  With that in mind, I have to address a few elements of 50 in order to justly review Day's latest. While the actual writing of 50 Shades was appalling in many spots (seriously, how many times can one \\"clamber?\\", did James forget that people don't \\"fetch\\" the phone in America, and don't get me started on Ana's underdeveloped eating disorder...), but with that being said, I FELT the characters.  I felt Christian's trauma and understood his need for control.  I felt Ana's naivete and willingness to make allowances because of her need for Christian.  I felt their attraction/need/desire for each other.  All their romping and various other bedroom/red room of pain/hotel acts made sense to me.  They added to the story.  Heck, I even understood Kate's character change throughout the series, and she was merely a supporting character.  Despite all of the flaws in James's virgin novel (pun intended), I still think she did a better job at creating a raw story line than seasoned author Sylvia Day, though Day's story is not to be discarded.<br /><br />***Note, MINOR spoilers ahead.  Nothing that should detract from your reading, but a few casual mentions of plot throughout***<br />Let's start with some positives...<br />Writing style:<br />Day is a veteran and professional author.  One look at the sheer number of books she's written and one can see she churns books out like butter.  She knows what she is doing...no doubt about that.  Bared to You vastly benefits from the professional writing.  The description in on point.  The action is well fleshed out.  The dialogue is realistic.  And repetition is minimal.  I didn't find myself cringing over amateur writing or rolling my eyes as a new word was used and then used again, and then again, as I did in James's novels.  Kudos to Day for excellent command of the written word.<br /><br />Setting:<br />Day obviously knows/understands New York City.  She doesn't spend pages and pages devoted to describing every detail of everything around (yawn), but she does give enough glimmers to make us believe she's familiar.  Night clubs, lounges, restaurants, even penthouse buildings all fit the bill for upscale Manhattan.<br /><br />Character development through dialogue:<br />I thought that the communication between Eva and Gideon was impeccable.  The characters seemed so REAL in the moments when they were going back and forth between their various issues and discussing strategies to attempt to move on.  I thought Day captured the real ebb and flow of relationship discussion in these pages.  Eva came across as someone who had obviously sought and used therapy and understood her issues to a tee, while Gideon was effectively painted as the character not quite as familiar with his own trauma.  Despite Eva's understanding of her triggers, etc., she didn't always do the \\"right\\" or \\"logical\\" thing, adding to the realism of the situation.  How many times do we think, \\"This is not a good idea\\" before making a decision, but do it anyway?  I definitely connected with the characters during their conversations more than any other time in the book.<br /><br />Possession:<br />Some reviewers have complained that Gideon's behavior is \\"stalker\\" and way unacceptable.  I didn't so much have a problem with that.  Yes, 21 missed calls in a 30 minute time span is a lot.  But, it's frantic, as was he.  Checking into someone's life without their permission might seem invasive, but come on, we live in a world of facebook, twitter, linkedin, google, etc.  With so many ways to gain information, cyber-stalking has become almost par for the 20-something crowd.  I know I've been guilty... As far as the flowers (if you haven't read, you'll understand if you do) I thought they were sweet.  She was trying to achieve an emotion in Gideon that she thought would help him and she went for it.  I didn't have a problem with this.<br /><br />Side characters:<br />While one reviewer criticized Day for providing a cast of physically perfect characters, I didn't have a problem with this.  We are in the socialite, upscale world of Manhattan after all.  One need only turn on Real Housewives of ___________ to determine that with a lot of wealth comes a lot of \\"beauty.\\"  I thought Day did a great job of capturing the selfish/caring vision of Eva's mom by realistically painting her as feeling like the \\"victim\\" in a situation even though she didn't suffer the actual trauma.  I thought Eva's reactions to her hyper-protective tendencies further enhanced the mother daughter dynamic.  I also felt like Cary is a character with quite a bit of intrigue left.  I hope Day shares with us more about his past.<br /><br />Some Issues...<br />Gideon and Eva:<br />While the dialogue of these two is excellent, I somehow missed their magnetic connection.  I felt like Day skipped over a few key parts of any relationship development (even jaw-dropping, love-at-first-sight ones.)  It seems to me like E and G went from electric chance meeting in the lobby to indecent proposal to total, intimate love-making in a limo.  I'm not sure I believed it.  As their relationship went on, I just decided to forego my doubts of the timeline and immediate falling in love and pretend they had developed more realistically as a couple.  This allowed me to better \\"get\\" their relationship throughout.<br /><br />Sexual Abuse Pasts:<br />I thought Day painted Eva in a mature and realistic way, as I've already said.  I believed her past.  I believed her internal dialogue as she clinically recognized triggers and appropriate/inappropriate responses, and I thought Day did a service to women by creating an abuse survivor who had a strong handle on herself and her surroundings.  There are very few times when Eva acts victimized, and even in those moments, she shows strength.  Gideon, on the other hand, is another story.  I didn't feel like his trauma was developed at all!  Now I know that we haven't even gotten the tip of the iceberg with his past, but it seemed like his \\"need\\" for control, his preference for brunettes, and his aversion to his childhood home are all said to be there, but I didn't see Day adhering to the writer's mantra of \\"show don't tell.\\"  Other than a few poignant night traumas, I didn't believe Gideon's issues like I did Eva's.  I hope that Day will improve this throughout the series.  For all of James's faults, I definitely believed Christian as a flawed character.<br /><br />Sex:<br />The sex scenes were fine.  I didn't think they did a whole lot to add to the story, but they also weren't gross or overly repetitive.  I mean, it's not normal for someone to have \\"a handful of orgasms,\\" but again, we don't want to read stories about people who have nice, average, loving sex 1-2 times a week as many/most married couples do.  This is, afterall, an escape from reality.<br /><br />Over-the-top:<br />This is a bit nitpicky, but the last issue I want to throw out there is the sheer over-the-topness of Gideon.  He is CONSTANTLY described as the most perfect, hot, sexy, fit, blah blah blah man EVER, but we don't really see him using his power in the business world like we did with Christian.  Finally, I thought Day could have better made us believe that he's a billionaire.  We don't really know how he made his money.  If he were 40 years old, then fine, we can assume he developed into a financier over time and there wouldn't be as big of a need to understand his success.  But he is 28 years old.  That means that in 6 years (assuming he was 22 when he graduated from Columbia) he has made $12 BILLION.  Oh, and in that time he's managed to buy a hotel, a chain ofgyms, at least one high end apartment building, at least two nightclubs, a majority party of a record company, oh and a huge luxury office building that is well over 20 stories high.  This is where his character somewhat fell apart for me.  I get the fantasy of the rich playboy.  I get that this is a romance novel and that no one wants to read the story of two average, middle-class people who fall in love and do average, middle-class things.  BUT, Day could have at least tried to let us believe Gideon's wealth by either toning it down a bit, or giving us more insight to his success.  Knowing he came from money, even giving some background that maybe he inherited a trust that got him started, etc. etc. would have been better than, \\"he has a really strong work ethic and as consequently bought half of Manhattan.\\"  This definitely caused some eye rolling on my end.  While Christian's wealth is no less over-the-top, James at least attempted to give us some clarity on how it was accumulated.<br /><br />Overall:<br />I like this book.  I will probably read the next two and hope that some of the issues resolve.  I just didn't have the same WOWSA, Best Book Ever! reactions that many reviewers did, and while I thought the writing was better, I still found myself thinking Eva and Gideon's relationship is a poor man's substitute to Ana and Christian's relationship.  Yes Eva is a better female lead.  Yes Christian is a better male lead.  Now, I just wonder what would happen if the two of them got together...

B0041KKYGU|3.0|As a comedy the movie does contain some genuine laugh-out-loud moments. However, anyone who was not terribly impressed with the commercials should be forewarned that the film is equally unimpressive. We begin with Elizabeth Halsey (Cameron Diaz,) a beauty who is beginning to feel her youth slipping away, and who attempts to fill the void in her life through drugs and shopping. Teaching was a profession which she fell into more so than anything, and consequently her attitude toward education is one of apathy. After a rocky beginning (her husband and his mother kick Elizabeth out of the house) she finds herself trading a sports car for a rice burner, as she drive drives back to JAMS (John Adams Middle School,) where, ironically enough, she had quit teaching three months before, having only been there for a year. Needless to say, her return to JAMS is not exactly thrilling to Elizabeth. She begins showing random movies which have class room scenes but which contain no educational qualities; all the while she is slumped on the side of her desk sleeping or using marijuana, or gazing with lusty eyes at the enlarged breasts of a supermodel. She herself, by the way, is squeezing every penny in the hopes having the operation done on her, which includes almost embezzling money from the middle school car wash.<br /><br />It doesn't take long for a zany teacher named Amy Squirrel to get on Elizabeth's bad side, with superficially cutesy but threatening comments like \\"I know that last year you skated by doing the who 'bare minimum thing...' but this year I just know we'll be more than across-the-hall-mates.\\" One the one hand, Amy is a stereotypical Hollywood portrayed educator who uses touchy-freely talk to work out problems and help students. Being somewhat eccentric, however, she treats faculty in much the same way that she treats her students, a fact which Elizabeth, who is brisk and businesslike, quickly comes to resent. What's more, Amy has a dark side, and underneath her I-feel-your-pain sympathies is a fiercely competitive and somewhat mentally unhinged teacher. Curiously enough, despite their very different world views, the two have in common their strong will and ruthless, Machiavellian methods of making each other miserable and using every one else as pones to undermine each other. So when a substitute math teacher Scott Delacourte (Justin Timberlake) who also happens to come from a well-to-do family in the watch making business, a classic romance triangle forms and the two women try everything to win him over. Scott himself is an offbeat character, embodying certain feminine features which, when played by Timberlake, are both  amusing and down right disturbing. Additionally Scott is weak and easily swayed, thus allowing for plenty of manipulation by both Amy and Elizabeth. On the outside of the triangle is Russel Gettis (Jason Segel,) a gym teacher at JAMS who, despite his crude comments and stalker-esque behavior, actually has feelings for Elizabeth, as is later shown by the fact that he thinks her body is just fine and that she doesn't need the operation to make her look beautiful.<br /><br />Elizabeth gets into plenty of sticky situations throughout the school year which will give the audience something to laugh about, including stealing Amy's theater class Little Orphan Annie wig and pretending to be a reporter to gain access to the forthcoming state scholastic tests (and getting a state official drunk,) speeding out of the school parking lot while blaring Black Sabbath, rubbing poison oak on an apple as a \\"gift\\" for Amy, and much more. Additionally, she wears, as Amy says \\"provacative beach ware,\\" to the middle car wash, looking like one of the beauties posing for Sports Illustrated more than a teacher at a car wash. We also meet an assortment of other characters, including Elizabeth's odd roommate who she later uses to blackmail the aforementioned state official. There is also the JAMS principal, who has a special place inhis heart (and office!) for all things dolphin, thus playing on the stereotype of the progressive, bleeding heart principal.<br /><br />Yet, as with all somewhat substantive comedies, the movie has its heartfelt moments, too, echoing the same limited substance of the American Pie franchise in its both tender and serious moments. We see that there are times when Elizabeth feels a connection with some of her students, particularly near the end of the movie, in which we see that she isn't a bad apple after all, despite her shortcomings and bad choices in so many areas. In her own way, she brings her own brand of realism to the table, helping some of the kids re-assess themselves. One of her students, Sasha, is a people-pleaser whose parents pressure her and desire that she aspire to high political office. Though Elizabeth's advice that Sasha should look into massage therapy instead is a bit heavy-handed, uncalled for, and cynical, the scene demonstrates Elizabeth's sense regarding the differences between hopes and dreams on the one hand, and real prospects on the other. Another example in which demonstrates she is not altogether apathetic about her students is seen in her lecture to one of the boys who has had an unrequited and hopeless crush on a classmate, Chance. It is here, perhaps for the first time, that Elizabeth demonstrates a disgust for her own superficial attempts to land marriage and money with Scott. She sees in herself the egoism of Chance, and she admonishes the male student to look for someone who can really appreciate his sensitive soul and interest in poetry. Additionally, about halfway through the school year she learns of a scheme to make her breast augmentation dreams a reality, via the teacher's bonus competition which comes from student's getting the highest scores on the annual state test. In one of the more touching elements about this film, we see an ever so subtle shift in Elizabeth's priorities as her interest becomes one of actually educating students rather than merely going through the motions.A final showdown with Amy occurs, and there are plenty of laughs to be had with that alone.<br /><br />But all and all the movie was not successful. The acting was a up to par as one can expect from comedies, and Cameron Diaz really played her role well. But as a whole, while the movie did contain some redeeming elements, most viewers will find the unorthodox behavior and pervasive foul language use of the teachers in front of their students, simply too much. There is a scene in which Elizabeth writes \\"stupid\\" on some papers, and while this will arouse laughter from some quarters, others will not be so amused. One final interesting aspect of the movie is that it is a parody in some ways of the American school system. Pperhaps well apart from the intentions of its creators, it shows that despite the cliche idea of teachers as nurturing, progressive, and altruistic, they are often regular people who gripe about going to the office, are self-interested, and look to break up the monotony in life by resorting to the very juvenile behavior they are thought to be above.<br /><br />Bad Teacher gets a C+ from me.

030758836X|5.0|GONE GIRL by Gillian Flynn is easily one of the best books I've read in a long time. The opening act is awesome. It delivers a compelling, dramatic, suspenseful scenario that will have you taking time out of your busy day to read the novel.<br /><br />Up front, you should know the chapter titles give away some of the story. However, the overall structure of the book is what makes this book great. Each chapter alternates point of view, first from husband Nick, then from his wife Amy. This is a novel about a wife going missing. Chapter one begins with Nick telling about the day of. Then chapter two is Amy's diary beginning back when she first met Nick seven years ago. As we read, Flynn hooks the reader into Nick's plight as he is investigated, implicated and scorned for his suspected involvement in his wife's disappearance. But, did Nick actually have anything to do with Amy's disappearance? This is where the great writing comes through. The evidence certainly points to Nick, but enough clues are given to where we don't think he did it, yet through Amy's diary entries, we know NIck isn't that great of a guy because Amy felt threatened by him.<br /><br />The set up for this novel is great. The thing about a husband and wife conflict is that it really could happen to anyone. So, it's easy to imagine yourself in a relationship gone bad. Also, Flynn writes as to make the possibilities of what happened to Amy endless. Was she murdered? Did Nick do it? Was she alive? Where was she? Why did the evidence point to NIck? What was he hiding? Will they even get back together.<br /><br />Ultimately, once the second half of the book begins, many questions get answered. I have to give Flynn credit for the surprising twists and turns she inserts into this cat and mouse game between husband and wife. Considering the great set up in the first part of the novel, I believe the second half really holds up, and is just as good. Flynn is a really good writer and I can't wait to read her previous two novels.vious two novels.

But I just finished reading it, and I have to tell you, it is a really interesting, entertaining and funny book.<br /><br />If you are musical, his discussions of his signature, open five-string tuning, and his personal influences and idols are great. If you like surprises, you'll love that he was both a choirboy (literally) and a Boy Scout (literally.)<br /><br />I mostly enjoyed his explanation of the songwriting process they went through (he and Mick.) Really interesting stuff. Keith would normally write up the riffs and give it a title or idea, then hand it off. Mick could scrawl out a song per hour sometimes. Keith wrote some songs in his sleep. And the songs are almost never really about what you might think they were about. \\"Jumpin' Jack Flash\\" was inspired by a stomping gardener named Jack who once woke up the \\"Glimmer Twins\\" who were sleeping late at Keith's house. \\"Flash\\" was just a word Mick thought sounded cool when he sang it.<br /><br />One of my favorite lines from the book comes when he is visiting his childhood neighborhood, and the local girls ask him, \\"Did you ever imagine...?\\"<br /><br />He replies, \\"I imagined everything. I never thought it would happen.\\"<br /><br />I highly recommend it.

Suddenly, the neighborhood is set upon by some mean aliens plummeting down from the skies. The boys manage to kill one which ticks the other aliens off to no end. So they are going to wreck some major havoc and try to kill pretty much anything that moves. Our female victim tags along with her tormentors to hopefully survive the situation and maybe even make things square. The authorities enter the fray to get pretty much everything wrong.<br /><br />Unlike the above mentioned films, this has no cute kids as heroes. These guys, though maybe not as tough as they think they are, have much more street savvy. They aren't out to survive the situation. They're out to prevail. Moses (played by John Boyega) is the leader of the gang. Yes he's tough, but you come to realize he's in a struggle to exist in a tough society. Sam (played by Jodie Whittaker) gets to see that Moses is not the one-dimensional evil-doer he he makes himself out to be. The rest of the crew are cool and funny, but not the cutesy funny of \\"Goonies\\" or \\"Gremlins.\\" They display street humor born of poor education and misinformation. 0307730697|5.0|Biographies have been a solid staple of my reading diet for the past few months.  Looking deep into the lives of great people is educational, inspiring, and helps me become more of the person that I'm created to be.  For most of my life, I dreaded reading the biographies that was assigned to read, or given by friends and families.  But recently, I have discovered that learning from the lives of others has incredible benefits for my life.<br /><br />Last night, in tears, I finished [[ASIN:0307730697 Fearless: The Undaunted Courage and Ultimate Sacrifice of Navy SEAL Team SIX Operator Adam Brown]], a biographical work on the life of Navy SEAL Adam Brown.  But the term \\"biographical\\" doesn't describe what this book really is.  Fearless is a book about a man, displaying his failures as well as his successes, his patriotism, his courage, his love for his family and for his God.  Fearless is better described as \\"inspirational\\", \\"motivational\\", and \\"life-changing\\".<br /><br />Fearless is the story of Adam Brown, a home-grown boy from Hot Springs, Arkansas.  The story details his life and activities as a child and teenager, his descent into drug abuse after high school, and his climb out of that pit into the elite world of specialized Navy warfare as a member of Navy SEAL Team SIX.<br /><br />Fearless is inspirational.  Reading this story will encourage you to higher levels of love and service, for your family, your friends, your country.  The life of Adam Brown will inspire you to love more, like Christ did.<br /><br />Fearless is motivational.  As you read of the injuries, the challenges, the setbacks, that Adam Brown overcame, you'll quickly feel the confidence that you can achieve your dreams as well.<br /><br />Fearless is life-changing. His passion for his wife and children was overwhelming.  His comradeship with his fellow teammates was deep and enduring.  His compassion for the poor and oppressed Afghan children was incredible.  And his desire to live for his Savior was awe-inspiring.<br /><br />Adam Brown was living proof that \\"with God, all things are possible.\\"  He lived his faith for all to see.<br /><br />Fearless was so captivating that it was difficult to put it down, even though I knew how it had to end. B005LAIIS0|3.0|Director Joe Carnahan made such a promising and exciting start in the feature film business with the 70's crime drama throwback NARC. It was strong, exciting and a shot in the arm to a tiring genre. He followed that up with the bizarre yet oddly entertaining ensemble actioner SMOKING ACES, but once locked into the Hollywood machine, he did the unnecessary but not-too-terrible film version of the 80's TV show THE A-TEAM, which teamed him for the first time with strong dramatic actor who became rebranded as action star Liam Neeson. This somehow led to THE GREY, a film that is not the survival adventure promised in the previews. THE GREY is an occasionally thoughtful, extremely existental, and ultimately predictible Man Versus Nature drama.<br /><br />We have John Ottway (Neeson, finally being allowed to speak in his natural accent), a gruff and suicidally depressed \\"exterminator\\" of sorts who works on an oil-drilling site in Alaska. His job essentially consists of picking off nasty man-eating wolves that look to use the workers as kibble with his rifle. He ponders poetically about his wife and how he misses her and how he knows he'll never be with her again. The other workers are, for the most part, societal outcasts; they're ex-cons, drifters, quick to anger and violence, meaning that Ottway is right at home with them. But it's time for them to go home, so they catch a plane back to Anchorage. There are warnings of a storm, or as co-writers Carnahan and Ian Mackenzie Jeffers (who wrote the short story this film is based on) call it, foreshadowing. The plane naturally crashes (in an uncomfortably effective set piece) and Ottway finds himself taking charge of a ragged and motley crew of survivors and look to stay alive amongst the harsh weather of the Alaska's snowy wastelands. Before too long, it seems that Ottway's work has followed him to the crash site as they soon find themselves surrounded by a pack of vicious ravening wolves. So instead of this being a story about men coming together and fighting the elements, it becomes something of a horror film as the men begin getting picked off one by one as Ottway tries to lead them to safety.<br /><br />Despite the film's massive leaps of logic and sense in order to manufacture forward momentum, it's an interesting film to watch. Carnahan does a commendable job of showing the brutal and unforgiving climate and also shows some beautiful vistas of the Alaskan wilderness. He's also pretty good at creating suspense, but it's really just enough to keep you watching despite knowing just about everything that's going to happen. You know that the film will become allegorical at one point for the behavior of the animals and the behavior of the men. You know there will be someone that will eventually challenge Ottway's \\"alpha\\" status, and that comes in the form of Diaz (the most interesting character of the film as played by a very volatile Frank Grillo, a great character actor). The whole cast of survivors, including Dallas Roberts and a very unrecognizable Dermot Mulroney, essentially seem to be analogues of the cast of John Carpenter's arctic existential horror masterpiece THE THING, but even that's not a terribly new idea. Survival thrillers often have the same archetypal characters. The film also delves into the nature of humanity's faith and the existence of a higher power, and this is where the film succeeds at being surprising yet unnecessarily ham-fisted as it becomes the love-child of Jack London and Samuel Beckett by way of Carnahan's profanity-and-gore-laden narrative. Neeson does nice work here and it further cements his current bad-ass status, but this is not even as interesting or as memorable of a role as Bryan Mills in TAKEN.<br /><br />Where I think the biggest disconnect is between audiences and this film is, exists in the realm of its marketing. This film promises certain things in the trailers and the commercials but doesn't live up to them, and the most glaring example of that is in the film's climax which is the very end of the film. While the moment promised to us is hardly the point of the film, it's something that everyone wanted to see, and when that was taken away from us, we felt cheated. There's truth to that. Most of the negative reviews specifically have to do with the film's ending and how it didn't live up to some very specific expectations, and that's more to do with the marketing than the film, because this is a film where the narrative isn't dependent on a climactic showdown between Ottway and the wolves; it's a more contemplative film than that. So I just don't think that we can blame Carnahan for the ending not meeting those expectations; Universal wanted to get butts in seats and what better way to do that with a film like this than the promise of Liam Neeson taping broken glass to his fists to do battle with a wolf?<br /><br />One way or the other, the biggest problem with THE GREY is that it attempts to be a thought-provoking piece of the existental crisis of Man Versus Nature, but because it's more structured like a survival horror film, it ends up not being terribly successful in either way.<br /><br />3/5 Stars.f that is in the film's climax which is the very end of the film. 6304712936|5.0|There are AFI top 100 movies that I watch once because I feel I should. There are movies I watch several times because they're involving. And then there are movies I own because they are immensely powerful. Amadeus is definitely one that I own, and one that I highly encourage others to watch several times if not own.<br /><br />Nearly 3 hours long, Amadeus is a work of art. There are numerous of Mozart's works on display here, not only with the gorgeous music, but put into a context. You can see why Mozart created these works and how they were received. You see the sets, see the environments they were put on within. Yes, you can argue about whether Mozart had that kind of laugh or whether he behaved the way the movie claims he did. But that's all beside the point. We'll never really know. What we DO know is the fantastic works of art he created, and how they are experienced when put on by a high quality orchestra, presented in full sound and color.<br /><br />What's amazing is that the main actor here, F. Murray Abraham, isn't even playing Mozart. He's the antagonist, Salieri, whose only desire in life is to create beautiful music for God. When he sees how short his talents are compared with Mozart, and what a disappointment Mozart is in so many other ways, he makes it his quest in life to take Mozart down. What follows is heartbreaking.<br /><br />There are so many little touches in the story that catch at you. The way Mozart is oblivious to how he's hurting Salieri when he has fun embellishing the march. To him it's simple child's play to make the march more interesting. The way he has fun playing upside down and backwards. He enjoys his gift. He feels lonely that few understand him.<br /><br />On the down side, there were many important women involved in this situation who are barely touched on. Salieri's obsession is a talented opera singer. And then there's Mozart's wife. She keeps him afloat, takes action to save the family at several points, and yet the ONLY time in the entire movie that a woman talks with another woman is when Mozart's wife briefly asks her new maid (Cynthia Dixon of Sex in the City fame) to please step outside. B0006GANN2|5.0|The French Connection is an amazing, true-life story which earned a #70 spot on the AFI Top 100 of movies for many reasons. One is that it features a car chase thought to be one of the best in cinema history. A car chases a subway trail through the streets of New York City, nearly getting destroyed along the way. The storyline is that of two NYC detectives who happen on a drug transaction involving a Frenchman. The detectives get grief from within the force, but with perseverence they are able to track down the perpetrators.<br /><br />Watching this in modern times is fascinating. First, there's a lot of throwing around of slurs about just about every ethnic group you can imagine. There is police brutality, slurs against women, against blacks, against Italians, against French, and it's all considered as normal. There's pretty much only one woman in the entire movie and I think she has two lines total. A cop shoots a suspect in the back and it's presented as typical. I do want to note that research shows that the police advisors to the movie complained about that scene and were overruled. But we definitely commented on that when watching it. And we do have NYC police in our family.<br /><br />It's interesting to watch how much life has changed in other ways. A guy goes to a ticket counter at an airline and buys a ticket with cash, and writes his own name on the ticket to take to the gate. In a subway, someone knocks on the driver's door and he simply opens it to see what they want. Nobody else seems to be able to stop the train - there were no emergency stop lines. And of course in modern times people would be tweeting and calling from the train, not completely cut off.<br /><br />I loved the scene where a drug dealer is being trailed and he's able to shake off the cop simply by getting on and off a subway. 055338466X|3.0|I first read A Brief History of Time by Stephen Hawking in grade school, then later after garnering (to one degree or another) a decent understanding of the basic principles of physics and the universe. My level of understanding increased as I repeated my sojourn into the text as well as with age, experience, and studies. I was captivated and made physics, especially the quantum variety, something of an odd hobby. I rarely shared this passion with anyone...at least not until much later when \\"Bazinga\\" became a recognizable and more socially acceptable punch line.<br /><br />I only recently decided to dive into The Grand Design. My reading list is long, and not all hobbies and passions can be realized and explored at the same time (well, maybe they can in the multiverse concept). And what is time anyway? I will admit at the onset that this book did mess with my head. I remember upon completion (the same day I was becoming familiar with Krauss's new book about `nothing') I could barely sleep, and went to bed with complex ideas concerning how one really could divide by zero. I do have nerd issues.<br /><br />Conceptually, I liked reading The Grand Design. However, the authors could have had the same effect in achieving purpose by publishing this work in much shorter form, perhaps a long entry in an academic journal, or a more reachable even shorter piece in a magazine...something like Popular Science. I'd shorten the first chapter, then weld it right alongside Chapter 6, skipping most of the middle and only applying conceptual explanation where absolutely necessary for reader comprehension. As an effective piece of literature, I'm sure the authors lost readers around Chapter 3 in many cases. The material in the middle chapters is technical and easily beyond the scope of the average reader's abilities in understanding. Dumbing down the language and rhetoric is doubtfully helpful. I know lay people picked up A Brief History of Time and could immediately find value; I justdon't see that happening with this text, especially if it's prone to readers only getting through the first couple of chapters. Maybe the target audience is the hobbyist, like me.<br /><br />I guess it just wasn't what I expected, and there is nothing really wrong with that. But marketing for this book (at least what I've seen thus far), seems to equate this to Hawking's earlier works--a false comparison. Also, and more importantly, I really expected to learn something new and abrupt. This book was more of a rehash of ideas that have been around for a decade or more. Many authors have dealt with the something from nothing idea, M-theory, and more. I did enjoy the concept of the Game of Life, my sole `new' finding during my read. When I finished, I wanted more, but not in the literarily curious way, but in the way of answers. I didn't get many.<br /><br />If you do decide to read the book, I recommend the paper version as opposed to eBook. I read this on my Kindle, and it lacked what I saw in the store, beautiful glossy pictures to help along the way. Instead, I got miniatures in black and white. Perhaps it's time to update to the Fire.<br /><br />(3 out of 5 stars as I did get something out of the read and enjoy the authors as scientists...but, probably more of a 2 in terms of literary value)
0345803485|1.0|A common notion among writers is that all writing is autobiographical.  When I read a novel, I try to imagine what kind of person would write such a thing.<br /><br />When I was in school, there was a certain type of girl who was often invited to a certain type of party.  The boys hosting these parties were the popular set- athletes, rich kids, good lookers, etc.  Their problem was that their A-list girlfriends were saving it for marriage. 0061367036|4.0|Apart from a few pictures of some of these monsters as realized in the series, the book is of great interest because it gives a rather exhaustive list of these monsters and their descriptions. They are presented in chapters that differentiate spirits from monsters; ghouls, revenants etc from witches, familiars, and black dogs (the page of these is not specified in the table of contents) and from demons. Two appendixes give alphabetical indexes of herbs, oils and hoodoo hands on one hand, and the names and attributes of European demons on the other hand.<br /><br />We can wonder why non-European demons are not also treated in an appendix. We can wonder why the beings are not treated in alphabetical order in the various chapters. But those are details, aren't they? Just like the fact there is no index.<br /><br />But don't be surprised if the book only looks at the demonic sides of things. Indians are reduced to curses only connected with burying grounds, though in the series when an Indian curse is used it is not to protect a burying ground but to get some kind of vengeance for a massacre of Indians by white settlers who wanted to take their land. You could be justified in thinking this book is biased.<br /><br />The Woman in White is universal, says the book. Is she really universal? The haunting woman who is walking the earth, crying and at times avenging herself for some kind of injustice is very common, but we could consider it as a mythological archetype that has vastly survived in our modern consciousness that makes us believe a woman, particularly a crying mother with babe is innocent. In the miniseries \\"The Pacific\\" an example is given in the last episodes of a civilian Japanese woman with a baby, trying to give the baby to the American soldiers around her and attracting them, at the same time diverting their attention, with this situation and when enough of them are around her she detonates the explosives she was carrying around her waist. Innocent you said?<br /><br />And it is the same thing in \\"Supernatural\\". The woman in white, or Bloody Mary, or whatever other woman who was the victim of some injustice, or who had committed some crime, in both cases we have injustice or crime followed by the death of the woman, these women are looking for justice in blind vengeance on some people who get caught in their haunting traps. It is effective as for suspense and horror and also as for our sense of justice being fulfilled, the liberation of the haunting spirit being achieved.<br /><br />But the archetype is not caught. This goes back to the emergence of Homo Sapiens from the primates before him. The premature state of the human baby requires two years of breast feeding and three to five years of very close care. That determined a clear division of labor along a sexual line: women looked after the children and carried them. They were either carrying a baby or infant that they breast-fed, or carrying a future baby in their womb. That's where this archetype of the victimized mother and of our pity for a mother with babe comes from. It is as old as Homo Sapiens. This book does not go into such consideration.<br /><br />That makes the book a useful tool but for people who are just accumulating direct and superficial knowledge on the series, not a tool to go beyond and analyse why it speaks so deeply to us. But that's the limitation of the genre: a book for aficionados and not a book for intellectuals. Some will tell me this is only an entertaining series. Of course. The Iliad was also just plain entertainment. That does not prevent us, human beings and human minds, from asking the silly question: \\"But what is the meaning of all that gore, horror and terror?\\"<br /><br />That question is not answered by this book.<br /><br />Dr Jacques COULARDEAU<br /><br />And it is the same thing in \\"Supernatural\\". 0307341577|4.0|Gillian Flynn has quickly become one of those writers I have personally categorized as \\"special\\"--and that places her among Stephen King, John Irving, Margaret Atwood, and Wally Lamb in my highly idiosyncratic pantheon of favorite authors. I follow these writers' careers closely, and I read their works strictly in the order in which they're published--which is why I had to read this novel now, before I read \\"Gone Girl,\\" her latest, the one that has garnered widespread praise that I'm pretty sure has blown the lid off the secret that Flynn is one of the best at the craft of writing engrossing, non-supernatural thrillers.<br /><br />\\"Dark Places\\" is a fictional account of what can happen to a down-on-its-luck Midwestern family that gets trapped in the net of cultural paranoia. In this case, that paranoia involves suspected pedophilia and Satanism. Flynn very wisely chooses not to discredit these cultural mania that seemed to spread like wildfire in the 1980s, but she does exploit (and I mean that in the very best sense of the word) their power to propel persecution and create misfortune is those \\"dark places\\" that are especially susceptible to them.<br /><br />Libby Day, the not-very-likeable protagonist, survived the brutal mass slaying of her two older sisters and her mother in 1985. In 2008, Libby, now a screwed-up adult with an empty life, must confront her past and her role in the aftermath of the slayings, which led to her brother's imprisonment. Forced into the unlikely role of detective, she digs through her history to uncover a number of unsettling facts that eventually lead her to the sad and painful truth of what happened on that fateful night 23 years ago. In weaving this compelling tale, Flynn demonstrates that, as William Faulkner said, \\"The past is never dead. It's not even past.\\"

0553801473|1.0|SPOILER ALERT - although by this point in time that is certainly moot and they are \\"spoilers\\" no longer:<br /><br />Much and more has been written regarding Gorge R R Martin's epic saga \\"A Song of Ice and Fire\\".  The masses waited with baited breath for years on end for the publication of A Feast for Crows.  Many were more than generous to Martin for that installment with hope that it was a fluke, that Dance with Dragons would set things right.  After all, Feast MUST be mere world building, focusing on \\"secondary\\" characters, intro of a seemingly endless array of new POV's, there must be some point right?  A big payday to come?  Well, sad to say that is not the case.  A least not in Dance w/o Dragons.<br /><br />I originally read Dance over a year ago - being one of the many leal followers of the Great Ser - who had to have this massive tome in hand upon its release.  I read.  I became confused.  I pondered.  Something was wrong.  Obviously I missed something.  I reread the previous books - then reread Dance - NO NO something was still not right.<br /><br />Let us journey back in time ..... back to July 2011 ..... the \\"fantasy nerd\\" in me is excited.  The mere weight of the book is exhilarating, thumbing through the pages my excitement builds as I see my beloved characters are finally back:  Tyrion, Daenerys, Jon ......I begin to read ........<br /><br />Warging -  ok not sure but I know this must be a key plot device.<br /><br />Finally a Tyrion chapter, water after a long dry spell,  OMG did I say water .... Ok, ok don't panic yet, drunk, dealing with guilt over killing his father, anxiety over Tysha, wait, wait, he is stuffed into a barrel (we all know Gorge is the American Tolkien so maybe this is his homage to The Hobbit/LoTR????) ...ends up in Pentos with Illyrio<br /><br />Daeny next:  why do I feel like I am watching GOT on HOB.  Obligatory/unnecessary sex act entertainment, Daeny with her breast bared, looks like ruling in Meereen is not smooth sailing after all (no reference to Tyrion's boat trip intended at this point I didn't know what was in store on that front), a bunch of new names (Shahaz, Reznak, Hizhadr)..oh my, I am already confused and rereading.....<br /><br />Jon POV next and not a total mess yet, but still early in the game.<br /><br />Bran:  will he finally find/meet the three eyed crow?<br /><br />Sad to say but in IMHO this book just never gets going or gives us much in the way of plot/character progression.  As has been stated brilliantly by many other reviewers for every step forward, it seems we take two back.  Tyrion never gets to Meereen to join forces with Daeny.  Daeny accomplishes nothing in Meereen and is actually weaker at the end of the book as she finds herself back on the Dothraki Sea where she has flown on Drogon (yes, Daeny finally rides her dragon but only after they had been imprisoned or MIA for much of the book).  Jon does not cement leadership at the Wall in preparation for a confrontation with The Others (who also are MIA) and is actually \\"killed\\" by his own men.<br /><br />All in all a very sad disappointment for me.  For years I looked forward to a continuation of the epic fantasy story that Martin introduced so brilliantly in GOT and continued in COK and SOS.  I believed that FFC was an aberration or possibly a labor of love for characters Martin cared for more than I  (Brianna, the Martells, the Greyjoys etc.) but no, we received much and more of the same in DWD.  How long must we eat cold, watered down  neep soup.  We need a hearty meal, Mr. Martin.<br /><br />Many positive reviews have expressed their opinions that these are middle/set up books, that Martin is still \\"world building\\".  I must take a different stand here.  While Martin has always been descriptive in his story telling, he was still able to advance the plot and move the story and characters along.  He did not do that in Feast or Dance.  In my opinion as a humble reader, I think the books should never have been split.  It should have been one book with appropriate editing.  Yes, this is Martin's story but we are his customers and he would be nothing without us.  We are within our rights as purchases to critique what we have bought.  If Martin were giving his books away, publishing the story free of charge on his website, then to be critical of his work would be disingenuous but this is not the case.<br /><br />In conclusion this book just left me feeling sad.  Characters I was once excited to read about now have no interest to me.  They have been dumbed down to the point that they are now cartoonish.  After my reread of Dance I tried to go back to read GOT again to see if any of the magic could be recaptured.  It was not possible.  After about 100 pages I stopped reading, boxed up my hardcover editions and donated them to Goodwill which seemed kind of ironic as I no longer have any goodwill for Ser Martin.plit. B007NUP6GS|5.0|The great BWV catalog of Bach's music groups six shorter vocal pieces together as \\"the motets,\\" though scholars debate whether there may have been more of them, now lost. Unlike his major choral works and cantatas, there is no accompaniment provided for most of these, so there's no consensus about the use of instruments in their performance, either. All agree, however, that these brief pieces--apparently written for funeral or memorial services--display Bach's art in miniature. I always enjoy listening to the Monteverdi Choir, but I had to marvel at the lightness of some of the singing in this recording. (They make it sound so easy!) Although the music often seems to fly by, and there's a very dance-like feel to it, tempi are actually slower than Harnoncourt employs with a larger chorus. In addition to the canonical Six, Gardiner also includes \\"Ich lasse dich nicht,\\" a less intricate work. This comes in the usual SDG hardcover album with texts and essays.
